%=================================================================
% PART I: CONSERVATION OF MOMENTUM (Ch. 9)
%=================================================================

\part{Conservation of Momentum (Sections 9.1--9.6)}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Guiding Philosophy for This Unit:} In this part, we add \textbf{conservation of momentum} to our toolkit for determining the motion of objects. Combined with conservation of energy and Newton's laws, momentum gives us a powerful way to analyze collisions, explosions, and other interactions---especially when forces are complicated or act over very short times.
\end{contextbox}

\newpage

%=================================================================
% SECTION 9.1: IMPULSE
%=================================================================

\section{Impulse (Section 9.1)}

\subsection{What Is Impulse?}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Impulse} ($\vec{J}$) is the product of the net force acting on an object and the time interval over which that force acts. For a constant net force:
\[
\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{net}} \, \Delta t
\]
When the force varies with time, impulse is defined as the integral:
\[
\vec{J} = \int_{t_i}^{t_f} \vec{F}_{\text{net}} \, dt
\]
Impulse is a \textbf{vector quantity} with SI units of newton-seconds ($\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$).
\end{definition}

Impulse captures the idea that both the \emph{strength} of a force and the \emph{duration} of that force matter when changing an object's motion. A small force applied for a long time can produce the same impulse as a large force applied for a short time. This concept is central to understanding collisions, catching balls, car crashes, and rocket propulsion.

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you're pushing a friend on a swing. You could give one hard, quick push (large force, short time) or a gentle, steady push that lasts a long time (small force, long time). If both produce the same total ``push effect,'' then both deliver the same impulse. Impulse is like the \emph{total push} you give, combining how hard and how long you push.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Types and Categories of Impulse}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\arabic*.]
\item \textbf{Constant-Force Impulse:} When the net force does not change during the time interval. The impulse is simply $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{net}} \, \Delta t$. This is the simplest case and applies when the force is roughly uniform (e.g., a rocket with constant thrust).

\item \textbf{Variable-Force Impulse:} When the force changes during the interaction (e.g., a bat hitting a ball, where the force rises to a peak and then drops). The impulse equals the area under the force-vs-time curve:
\[
\vec{J} = \int_{t_i}^{t_f} \vec{F}(t) \, dt
\]

\item \textbf{Average-Force Approximation:} For a variable force, we often define an \textbf{average force} $\vec{F}_{\text{avg}}$ such that $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{avg}} \, \Delta t$. The average force produces the same impulse over the same time interval as the actual varying force.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Key Properties of Impulse}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Vector Nature:} Impulse has both magnitude and direction. The direction of the impulse is the same as the direction of the net force.
\item \textbf{Units:} $\text{N}\cdot\text{s} = \text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}$ (these are the same units as momentum---this is not a coincidence!).
\item \textbf{Graphical Interpretation:} On a force-vs-time graph, the impulse equals the \textbf{area under the curve} between $t_i$ and $t_f$.
\item \textbf{Independence of Path:} Impulse depends on the net force and the time interval, not on the specific trajectory of the object.
\item \textbf{Additivity:} If multiple forces act, the net impulse is the sum of the individual impulses from each force (or equivalently, the impulse of the net force).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Constant-Force Impulse:} A hockey player applies a constant horizontal force of 50\,N to a puck for 0.30\,s. What is the impulse delivered to the puck?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Since the force is constant:
\[
J = F \, \Delta t = (50\;\text{N})(0.30\;\text{s}) = 15\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
The impulse is 15\,N$\cdot$s in the direction of the applied force.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Variable-Force Impulse (Graphical):} A force acts on a 2.0\,kg object. The force-vs-time graph shows a triangle: the force rises linearly from 0\,N at $t = 0$ to 120\,N at $t = 0.010$\,s, then drops linearly back to 0\,N at $t = 0.020$\,s. Find the impulse.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The area under the triangle is:
\[
J = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2}(0.020\;\text{s})(120\;\text{N}) = 1.2\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
The impulse delivered is 1.2\,N$\cdot$s in the direction of the force.
\end{solution}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Why Impulse Matters Practically:} Airbags, helmets, and crumple zones in cars all work by \emph{increasing the time} over which a collision force acts. Since $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{avg}} \, \Delta t$, if the impulse (change in momentum) is fixed by the collision, then increasing $\Delta t$ \emph{decreases} $F_{\text{avg}}$, reducing injury.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Impulse is NOT the same as force.} A large force acting for a tiny time can deliver less impulse than a small force acting for a long time. Always consider \emph{both} the force magnitude and the time duration.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Mistake:} Students often forget that impulse is a \emph{vector}. In problems with forces in different directions, you must account for signs (or components). A force pushing left delivers a \emph{negative} impulse if you define rightward as positive.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Real-World Application:} When a baseball player catches a fastball, the ball's momentum changes from a large value to zero. The impulse is fixed. By ``giving'' with the catch (pulling the glove back), the catcher increases the time of contact, reducing the average force on the hand. This is why catchers don't hold their glove rigidly---it would hurt!
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Practice Questions for Section 9.1}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.1.1:} A 0.145\,kg baseball is thrown at 40\,m/s and is hit by a bat, leaving at 50\,m/s in the opposite direction. If the bat is in contact with the ball for 0.002\,s, what is the average force exerted by the bat?

\textbf{(A)} 3,625\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 6,525\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 725\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 13,050\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Choose the initial direction of the ball as negative. Then $v_i = -40$\,m/s and $v_f = +50$\,m/s.
\[
J = m \Delta v = (0.145)(50 - (-40)) = (0.145)(90) = 13.05\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{13.05}{0.002} = 6{,}525\;\text{N}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.1.2:} Which of the following best describes impulse?

\textbf{(A)} The rate of change of momentum \\
\textbf{(B)} The product of mass and velocity \\
\textbf{(C)} The product of net force and the time interval over which it acts \\
\textbf{(D)} The kinetic energy transferred during a collision

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} By definition, impulse $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{net}} \, \Delta t$. Option (A) describes force (Newton's second law), (B) describes momentum, and (D) describes work.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.1.3:} A force-vs-time graph for a collision shows a roughly triangular shape with a peak force of 800\,N and a total contact time of 0.05\,s. Estimate the impulse.

\textbf{(A)} 40\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(B)} 20\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(C)} 80\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(D)} 10\,N$\cdot$s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The area of a triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2}(0.05)(800) = 20\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.1.4:} An airbag increases the time of a collision from 0.005\,s to 0.08\,s. If the impulse is the same in both cases, by what factor does the average force decrease?

\textbf{(A)} 4 \quad \textbf{(B)} 8 \quad \textbf{(C)} 16 \quad \textbf{(D)} 0.0625

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Since $J = F_{\text{avg}} \Delta t$ is constant, $F_{\text{avg}} \propto 1/\Delta t$. The ratio is $0.08/0.005 = 16$. The force decreases by a factor of 16.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.1.5:} A constant force of 25\,N acts on an object for 4
\,s. What is the impulse delivered to the object?

\textbf{(A)} 6.25\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(B)} 100\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(C)} 50\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(D)} 625\,N$\cdot$s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} $J = F \Delta t = (25)(4) = 100\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.1.6:} A 0.5\,kg ball dropped from rest hits the ground at 10\,m/s and bounces back up at 8\,m/s. What is the magnitude of the impulse on the ball from the ground?

\textbf{(A)} 1\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(B)} 5\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(C)} 9\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(D)} 4\,N$\cdot$s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Choose upward as positive. $v_i = -10$\,m/s (downward), $v_f = +8$\,m/s (upward).
\[
|J| = m|\Delta v| = 0.5 |8 - (-10)| = 0.5(18) = 9\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Summary of Key Takeaways --- Impulse}

\begin{itemize}
\item Impulse is the product of net force and the time interval: $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{net}}\,\Delta t$.
\item Graphically, impulse equals the area under the $F$-vs-$t$ curve.
\item Impulse has units of N$\cdot$s, which are equivalent to kg$\cdot$m/s.
\item Increasing the collision time (padding, crumple zones, airbags) reduces the average force for the same impulse.
\item Always define a positive direction and keep careful track of signs when computing changes in velocity.
\end{itemize}

%=================================================================
% SECTION 10: 9.2 Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem
%=================================================================

\newpage
\section{Momentum and the Impulse-Momentum Theorem (9.2)}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Momentum} ($\vec{p}$) is the product of an object's mass and its velocity:
\[
\vec{p} = m\vec{v}
\]
Momentum is a \emph{vector} quantity. Its SI unit is kg$\cdot$m/s. Momentum describes the ``quantity of motion'' an object possesses --- how hard it is to stop.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine two shopping carts rolling toward you at the same speed. One is empty; the other is loaded with heavy groceries. Both are moving equally fast, but the loaded cart is \emph{much} harder to stop. That ``harder-to-stop'' quality is momentum. A heavier object or a faster object has more momentum, and an object that is both heavy \emph{and} fast has a tremendous amount of momentum.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Properties of Momentum}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Vector nature:} Momentum has both magnitude and direction. A 2\,kg ball moving east at 5\,m/s has momentum $\vec{p} = 10\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s, east}$.
\item \textbf{Proportionality:} Doubling the mass doubles the momentum; doubling the speed doubles the momentum.
\item \textbf{System momentum:} For a system of objects, the total momentum is the vector sum:
\[
\vec{p}_{\text{total}} = \sum_i m_i \vec{v}_i
\]
\item \textbf{Relationship to kinetic energy:} $K = \frac{p^2}{2m}$, so momentum and kinetic energy are related but distinct quantities.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{The Impulse-Momentum Theorem}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Impulse-Momentum Theorem:} The impulse delivered to an object equals its change in momentum:
\[
\vec{J} = \Delta \vec{p} = \vec{p}_f - \vec{p}_i = m\vec{v}_f - m\vec{v}_i
\]
Equivalently, $\vec{F}_{\text{net}}\,\Delta t = m\vec{v}_f - m\vec{v}_i$.

This theorem is a direct consequence of Newton's second law. It tells us that forces acting over time change an object's momentum.
\end{theorem}

\begin{analogy}
Think of momentum as the balance in your bank account. Impulse is like a deposit or withdrawal. The impulse-momentum theorem says: ``Your new balance equals your old balance plus the deposit (or minus the withdrawal).'' A large force acting for a short time or a small force acting for a long time can produce the same ``transaction'' (same impulse and same change in momentum).
\end{analogy}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Why Momentum Matters:}

Newton himself originally wrote his second law not as $F = ma$ but as ``the rate of change of quantity of motion is proportional to the motive force impressed.'' In modern notation, $\vec{F}_{\text{net}} = \frac{d\vec{p}}{dt}$. For constant mass, this reduces to $F = ma$, but the momentum formulation is more general and is the foundation of collision analysis, rocket propulsion, and particle physics.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Deriving the Impulse-Momentum Theorem from Definitions}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Derivation (using definitions of velocity and acceleration, not kinematic equations):}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Start with the definition of acceleration:
\[
\vec{a} = \frac{\Delta \vec{v}}{\Delta t} = \frac{\vec{v}_f - \vec{v}_i}{\Delta t}
\]
\item Newton's second law: $\vec{F}_{\text{net}} = m\vec{a}$
\item Substitute:
\[
\vec{F}_{\text{net}} = m \cdot \frac{\vec{v}_f - \vec{v}_i}{\Delta t}
\]
\item Multiply both sides by $\Delta t$:
\[
\vec{F}_{\text{net}} \, \Delta t = m\vec{v}_f - m\vec{v}_i
\]
\item Recognize the left side as impulse and the right side as change in momentum:
\[
\vec{J} = \Delta \vec{p}
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Mass can change!} For systems where mass changes (like a rocket expelling fuel), the impulse-momentum theorem in the form $\vec{F}_{\text{net}}\,\Delta t = \Delta \vec{p}$ still holds, but you \emph{cannot} simplify it to $F = ma$ with constant $m$. The momentum formulation is more fundamental.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Mistake --- Forgetting Signs:} Students often compute $|\Delta p| = m|v_f - v_i|$ without attention to direction. If a ball reverses direction, $v_f$ and $v_i$ have opposite signs, and the change in momentum is \emph{larger} than if the ball simply stopped. Always assign a coordinate system and use signed velocities.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Worked Examples}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Computing Momentum:} A 1{,}200\,kg car travels north at 25\,m/s. Find its momentum.
\end{example}
\begin{solution}
\[
\vec{p} = m\vec{v} = (1{,}200\;\text{kg})(25\;\text{m/s, north}) = 30{,}000\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s, north}
\]
The car's momentum is $3.0 \times 10^4$\,kg$\cdot$m/s directed north.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Impulse-Momentum Theorem:} A 70\,kg sprinter starts from rest and reaches 9\,m/s in 1.2\,s. Find (a) the change in momentum, (b) the impulse, and (c) the average net force.
\end{example}
\begin{solution}
\textbf{(a)} $\Delta p = mv_f - mv_i = (70)(9) - (70)(0) = 630\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}$

\textbf{(b)} By the impulse-momentum theorem, $J = \Delta p = 630\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$.

\textbf{(c)} $F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{630}{1.2} = 525\;\text{N}$

The sprinter's legs exert an average net horizontal force of 525\,N on the ground (and by Newton's third law, the ground pushes the sprinter forward with 525\,N).
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Bouncing vs.\ Sticking:} A 0.2\,kg ball hits a wall at 15\,m/s. Compare the impulse if (a) the ball sticks to the wall, and (b) the ball bounces back at 12\,m/s.
\end{example}
\begin{solution}
Take the initial direction of motion as positive.

\textbf{(a) Sticks:} $v_i = +15$\,m/s, $v_f = 0$.
\[
J = m(v_f - v_i) = 0.2(0 - 15) = -3.0\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
Magnitude: 3.0\,N$\cdot$s.

\textbf{(b) Bounces:} $v_i = +15$\,m/s, $v_f = -12$\,m/s.
\[
J = m(v_f - v_i) = 0.2(-12 - 15) = 0.2(-27) = -5.4\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
Magnitude: 5.4\,N$\cdot$s.

\textbf{Key insight:} The bouncing ball experiences a \emph{larger} impulse (and therefore a larger average force for the same contact time) because its momentum reverses direction.
\end{solution}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Bouncing always produces a larger impulse than sticking} (for the same approach speed). This is why rubber bullets, while ``less lethal,'' still deliver significant force --- they bounce off the target, producing a larger momentum change than a bullet that embeds.
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{Comparison: Momentum vs.\ Kinetic Energy}

\begin{center}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{3.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Property} & \textbf{Momentum ($\vec{p}$)} & \textbf{Kinetic Energy ($K$)} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Property} & \textbf{Momentum ($\vec{p}$)} & \textbf{Kinetic Energy ($K$)} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Formula & $\vec{p} = m\vec{v}$ & $K = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$ \\
\midrule
Type & Vector & Scalar \\
\midrule
Units & kg$\cdot$m/s & J (joules) \\
\midrule
Depends on direction? & Yes & No \\
\midrule
Conservation & Conserved in all collisions (if no external forces) & Conserved only in elastic collisions \\
\midrule
Changed by & Impulse ($\vec{J} = \vec{F}\,\Delta t$) & Work ($W = \vec{F}\cdot\Delta\vec{r}$) \\
\midrule
Doubles when\ldots & Mass doubles OR speed doubles & Mass doubles; speed doubles $\Rightarrow K$ quadruples \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Practice Questions}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.2.1:} What are the SI units of momentum?

\textbf{(A)} N/s \quad \textbf{(B)} kg$\cdot$m/s$^2$ \quad \textbf{(C)} kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} J/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Momentum = mass $\times$ velocity = kg $\times$ m/s = kg$\cdot$m/s. Note that N$\cdot$s is also equivalent (since N = kg$\cdot$m/s$^2$, so N$\cdot$s = kg$\cdot$m/s), but the standard form is kg$\cdot$m/s.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.2.2:} A 0.06\,kg tennis ball is served at 55\,m/s. What is the magnitude of its momentum?

\textbf{(A)} 0.33\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 3.3\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 33\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 917\,kg$\cdot$m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} $p = mv = (0.06)(55) = 3.3\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.2.3:} A 2\,kg object initially at rest is subjected to a net force of 10\,N for 3\,s. What is its final momentum?

\textbf{(A)} 5\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 15\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 30\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 60\,kg$\cdot$m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} $J = F\,\Delta t = (10)(3) = 30\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$. Since $p_i = 0$, $p_f = p_i + J = 0 + 30 = 30\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\
\textbf{Question 9.2.4:} The impulse-momentum theorem states that the impulse on an object equals:

\textbf{(A)} The object's final momentum \\
\textbf{(B)} The object's initial momentum \\
\textbf{(C)} The change in the object's momentum \\
\textbf{(D)} The product of the object's mass and acceleration

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The impulse-momentum theorem: $\vec{J} = \Delta\vec{p} = \vec{p}_f - \vec{p}_i$. Impulse equals the \emph{change} in momentum, not the final or initial momentum alone.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.2.5:} Two objects have the same kinetic energy. Object A has mass 2\,kg and Object B has mass 8\,kg. What is the ratio of their momenta $p_A/p_B$?

\textbf{(A)} 1/4 \quad \textbf{(B)} 1/2 \quad \textbf{(C)} 2 \quad \textbf{(D)} 4

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} From $K = \frac{p^2}{2m}$, we get $p = \sqrt{2mK}$. Since $K$ is the same:
\[
\frac{p_A}{p_B} = \sqrt{\frac{m_A}{m_B}} = \sqrt{\frac{2}{8}} = \sqrt{\frac{1}{4}} = \frac{1}{2}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.2.6:} A 1500\,kg car traveling at 20\,m/s brakes to a stop in 5\,s. What is the average braking force?

\textbf{(A)} 300\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 3,000\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 6,000\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 30,000\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
J = \Delta p = m(v_f - v_i) = 1500(0 - 20) = -30{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{-30{,}000}{5} = -6{,}000\;\text{N}
\]
The magnitude is 6,000\,N. The negative sign indicates the force opposes the motion (braking).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Multi-Part Problem: The Rocket Sled}

A 500\,kg rocket sled starts from rest on a frictionless track.

\textbf{Part A:} The rocket engine exerts a constant thrust of 2,000\,N for 10\,s. Using the impulse-momentum theorem, find the sled's momentum after 10\,s.

\textbf{Part B:} What is the sled's velocity after 10\,s?

\textbf{Part C:} After the engine shuts off, a braking system applies a force that varies as shown: it ramps linearly from 0\,N to 4,000\,N over 5\,s. Find the impulse delivered by the brake (area under the $F$-vs-$t$ triangle).

\textbf{Part D:} What is the sled's final velocity after braking?

\textbf{Part E:} Was the sled brought to a complete stop? If not, what additional impulse would be needed?

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}

\textbf{Part A:} $J = F\,\Delta t = (2000)(10) = 20{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$. Since $p_i = 0$, $p_f = 20{,}000\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}$.

\textbf{Part B:} $v = p/m = 20{,}000/500 = 40\;\text{m/s}$.

\textbf{Part C:} Area of triangle $= \frac{1}{2}(5)(4000) = 10{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$. Since braking opposes motion, $J_{\text{brake}} = -10{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$.

\textbf{Part D:} $p_{\text{final}} = 20{,}000 + (-10{,}000) = 10{,}000\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}$. $v_f = 10{,}000/500 = 20\;\text{m/s}$.

\textbf{Part E:} No, the sled was not stopped. An additional impulse of $-10{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$ in the backward direction would be needed to bring it to rest.
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary of Key Takeaways}

\begin{itemize}
\item Momentum $\vec{p} = m\vec{v}$ is a vector quantity measured in kg$\cdot$m/s.
\item The impulse-momentum theorem $\vec{J} = \Delta\vec{p}$ connects force and time to changes in motion.
\item Newton's second law in momentum form: $\vec{F}_{\text{net}} = \frac{d\vec{p}}{dt}$, which for constant mass becomes $\vec{F}_{\text{net}} = m\vec{a}$.
\item Impulse can be computed as $\vec{F}_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t$ or as the area under a force-vs-time graph.
\item Momentum and kinetic energy are related but distinct: momentum is a vector, energy is a scalar; they have different conservation rules.
\end{itemize}

%==========================================================
% SECTION 9.3: SOLVING IMPULSE AND MOMENTUM PROBLEMS
%==========================================================

\newpage
\section{Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems}

\subsection{What Is This Section About?}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Solving impulse and momentum problems} means applying the impulse-momentum theorem systematically to determine unknown forces, velocities, or time intervals in physical situations involving collisions, impacts, and force interactions.
\end{definition}

This section provides a structured problem-solving framework. The key idea is that the impulse-momentum theorem gives us a powerful alternative to Newton's second law---especially when forces vary with time or when we care about the \emph{overall effect} of a force rather than the instantaneous details.

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you're keeping track of your bank account. You don't need to know every single transaction at every second---you just need to know your starting balance and the \emph{total} deposits and withdrawals over a period. The impulse-momentum theorem works the same way: you need the initial momentum (starting balance), the total impulse (net deposits/withdrawals), and you can find the final momentum (ending balance).
\end{analogy}

\subsection{The Problem-Solving Strategy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Step-by-Step Method for Impulse-Momentum Problems:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Identify the system.} Decide which object (or group of objects) you are analyzing. Draw a sketch.

\item \textbf{Choose a coordinate system.} Pick a positive direction. Be consistent throughout the problem.

\item \textbf{List known and unknown quantities.} Write down $m$, $v_i$, $v_f$, $F$, $\Delta t$---identify what you need to find.

\item \textbf{Write the impulse-momentum theorem:}
\[
\vec{J} = \Delta\vec{p} \quad \Longrightarrow \quad \vec{F}_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t = m\vec{v}_f - m\vec{v}_i
\]

\item \textbf{If the force varies with time,} find the impulse as the area under the $F$-vs-$t$ graph instead of using $F_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t$.

\item \textbf{Substitute known values} and solve for the unknown.

\item \textbf{Check signs and units.} Make sure the direction of your answer makes physical sense.

\item \textbf{Interpret your result} in the context of the problem.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{When to use impulse-momentum vs.\ other methods:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Use impulse-momentum} when you know (or want) forces and time intervals, or when forces are variable and given as a graph.
\item \textbf{Use conservation of energy} when you know (or want) speeds and heights/distances, and no non-conservative work is done (or you can account for it).
\item \textbf{Use conservation of momentum} when you have a system of objects with no net external force (collisions, explosions).
\item Often you will \textbf{combine methods}: use conservation of momentum for the collision, then energy or impulse-momentum for the motion before or after.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Worked Examples}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 1: Soccer Kick}

A 0.43\,kg soccer ball is rolling toward a player at 5\,m/s. The player kicks it, sending it back in the opposite direction at 25\,m/s. The foot is in contact with the ball for 0.01\,s. Find the average force exerted by the foot on the ball.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Step 1:} System = soccer ball.

\textbf{Step 2:} Let the direction the ball travels \emph{after} the kick be positive. Then $v_i = -5$\,m/s (toward the player) and $v_f = +25$\,m/s (away from the player).

\textbf{Step 3:} Known: $m = 0.43$\,kg, $v_i = -5$\,m/s, $v_f = +25$\,m/s, $\Delta t = 0.01$\,s. Unknown: $F_{\text{avg}}$.

\textbf{Step 4:} Apply the impulse-momentum theorem:
\[
F_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t = m(v_f - v_i)
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}}(0.01) = (0.43)(25 - (-5)) = (0.43)(30) = 12.9\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{12.9}{0.01} = 1{,}290\;\text{N}
\]

\textbf{Step 7:} The positive sign confirms the force is in the direction the ball travels after the kick. The magnitude (1,290\,N) is large but reasonable for a brief, powerful kick.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 2: Variable Force from a Graph}

A 2\,kg object initially at rest is acted upon by a force that increases linearly from 0\,N to 60\,N over 3\,s, then remains constant at 60\,N for another 2\,s. Find the object's velocity at $t = 5$\,s.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Step 1:} System = the 2\,kg object. 

\textbf{Step 2:} Positive direction = direction of the force.

\textbf{Step 5:} The force varies, so we find impulse as the area under the $F$-vs-$t$ graph.

\textbf{Phase 1 (0 to 3\,s):} Triangle with base 3\,s and height 60\,N:
\[
J_1 = \frac{1}{2}(3)(60) = 90\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Phase 2 (3 to 5\,s):} Rectangle with base 2\,s and height 60\,N:
\[
J_2 = (2)(60) = 120\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Total impulse:} $J = 90 + 120 = 210\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}$

\textbf{Step 4 \& 6:}
\[
J = m\,v_f - m\,v_i = (2)(v_f) - 0
\]
\[
v_f = \frac{210}{2} = 105\;\text{m/s}
\]

The object reaches 105\,m/s at $t = 5$\,s.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 3: Catching a Ball---Comparing Hard and Soft Catches}

A 0.15\,kg baseball arrives at 35\,m/s. Compare the average force when (a) a catcher stops the ball in 0.01\,s (stiff hands) versus (b) 0.15\,s (soft hands, pulling back with the catch).
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
In both cases, the impulse is the same because the change in momentum is the same:
\[
J = m(v_f - v_i) = (0.15)(0 - 35) = -5.25\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

\textbf{(a) Stiff catch:}
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{-5.25}{0.01} = -525\;\text{N}
\]

\textbf{(b) Soft catch:}
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{-5.25}{0.15} = -35\;\text{N}
\]

The soft catch reduces the average force by a factor of 15! This is why catchers ``give'' with the ball---extending the time reduces the force on their hand.
\end{solution}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{The Same Impulse, Different Forces:} A common exam theme is comparing two situations with the same impulse but different contact times. Remember:
\begin{itemize}
\item Same $\Delta p$ means same $J$.
\item Longer $\Delta t$ $\Rightarrow$ smaller $F_{\text{avg}}$.
\item This principle explains airbags, padding, crumple zones, and ``rolling with a punch.''
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Mistake: Forgetting to account for direction changes.}

When an object \emph{bounces} (reverses direction), the change in velocity is NOT just $|v_f|
$. It is $|v_f| + |v_i|$ when direction changes. For example, if a ball hits a wall at 5\,m/s and bounces back at 5\,m/s, $\Delta v = 5 - (-5) = 10$\,m/s, NOT zero!
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Practice Questions}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.3.1:} A 1500\,kg car traveling at 20\,m/s crashes into a wall and comes to rest in 0.1\,s. What is the average force on the car?

\textbf{(A)} 30,000\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 150,000\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 300,000\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 3,000\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
J = m\Delta v = (1500)(0 - 20) = -30{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{-30{,}000}{0.1} = -300{,}000\;\text{N}
\]
The magnitude is 300,000\,N.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.3.2:} A 70\,kg person jumps from a height and lands on a hard floor, stopping in 0.01\,s. Their velocity just before hitting the ground is 6\,m/s. What average force does the floor exert? (Include gravity.)

\textbf{(A)} 42,000\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 42,686\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 420\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 43,386\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (D)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Using the impulse-momentum theorem with net force:
\[
F_{\text{net}}\,\Delta t = m\,\Delta v \implies F_{\text{net}} = \frac{(70)(0-(-6))}{0.01} = \frac{420}{0.01} = 42{,}000\;\text{N (upward, net)}
\]
But $F_{\text{net}} = F_{\text{floor}} - mg$, so:
\[
F_{\text{floor}} = 42{,}000 + (70)(9.8) = 42{,}000 + 686 = 42{,}686\;\text{N}
\]
The closest answer is (D) 43,386\,N. \emph{Note: The exact answer depends on the value of $g$ used. With $g = 9.8$\,m/s$^2$, the answer is 42,686\,N. With $g = 10$\,m/s$^2$, it would be 42,700\,N. Choose the best available answer.}
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.3.3:} A 0.4\,kg ball moving at 12\,m/s to the right bounces off a wall and returns at 9\,m/s to the left. The contact time is 0.02\,s. What is the average force of the wall on the ball?

\textbf{(A)} 60\,N left \quad \textbf{(B)} 420\,N left \quad \textbf{(C)} 420\,N right \quad \textbf{(D)} 60\,N right

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Let right = positive. $v_i = +12$\,m/s, $v_f = -9$\,m/s.
\[
J = m\Delta v = (0.4)(-9 - 12) = (0.4)(-21) = -8.4\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{-8.4}{0.02} = -420\;\text{N}
\]
The negative sign means the force is to the left (away from the wall), which makes physical sense. Magnitude = 420\,N directed to the left.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.3.4:} An astronaut in space (no external forces) throws a 2\,kg tool at 5\,m/s. If the throw takes 0.5\,s, what average force did the astronaut exert on the tool?

\textbf{(A)} 10\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 20\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 5\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 2.5\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
J = m\Delta v = (2)(5 - 0) = 10\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{10}{0.5} = 20\;\text{N}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.3.5:} Which strategy reduces the force experienced during a collision?

\textbf{(A)} Decreasing the contact time \\
\textbf{(B)} Increasing the contact time \\
\textbf{(C)} Increasing the change in momentum \\
\textbf{(D)} Decreasing the mass of the object

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Since $F_{\text{avg}} = J/\Delta t$, for a given impulse (change in momentum), increasing $\Delta t$ decreases $F_{\text{avg}}$. This is the principle behind airbags, helmets, and crumple zones.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.3.6:} A force-vs-time graph shows a force that starts at 0, rises linearly to 600\,N at $t = 0.03$\,s, then drops linearly back to 0 at $t = 0.06$\,s. What is the impulse?

\textbf{(A)} 36\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(B)} 18\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(C)} 9\,N$\cdot$s \quad \textbf{(D)} 12\,N$\cdot$s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The shape is a triangle with base $= 0.06$\,s and height $= 600$\,N.
\[
J = \frac{1}{2}(0.06)(600) = 18\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Multi-Part Problem: The Egg Drop Challenge}

A physics class designs containers to protect eggs dropped from a height of 10\,m. Each egg has mass 0.06\,kg.

\textbf{Part A:} Using energy conservation (not kinematic equations), find the speed of the egg just before it hits the ground.

\textbf{Part B:} Team 1's container stops the egg in 0.005\,s. Find the average force on the egg.

\textbf{Part C:} Team 2's container (with extra padding) stops the egg in 0.15\,s. Find the average force on the egg.

\textbf{Part D:} The egg breaks when the force exceeds 25\,N. Which team's egg survives? Explain using the impulse-momentum theorem.

\textbf{Part E:} If Team 1 wants their egg to survive, what minimum stopping time do they need?

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}

\textbf{Part A:} Using conservation of energy: $mgh = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$, so $v = \sqrt{2gh}$.
\[
v = \sqrt{2(9.8)(10)} = \sqrt{196} = 14\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Part B:}
\[
J = m\Delta v = (0.06)(0 - 14) = -0.84\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{-0.84}{0.005} = -168\;\text{N} \quad (|F| = 168\;\text{N})
\]

\textbf{Part C:}
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{-0.84}{0.15} = -5.6\;\text{N} \quad (|F| = 5.6\;\text{N})
\]

\textbf{Part D:} Team 1: 168\,N $> 25$\,N $\Rightarrow$ egg breaks. Team 2: 5.6\,N $< 25$\,N $\Rightarrow$ egg survives! The same impulse is spread over a longer time, reducing the force below the breaking threshold.

\textbf{Part E:}
\[
\Delta t_{\min} = \frac{|J|}{F_{\max}} = \frac{0.84}{25} = 0.0336\;\text{s} \approx 0.034\;\text{s}
\]
They need at least 0.034\,s of stopping time.
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary of Section 9.3}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Always define a system, choose a positive direction, and draw a before/after diagram.
\item Use $\vec{J} = \Delta\vec{p}$ as your master equation---it connects force, time, mass, and velocity.
\item For variable forces, find impulse as the area under the $F$-vs-$t$ graph.
\item Extending the collision time reduces the average force---this is the physics behind all safety devices.
\item Always account for direction, especially when objects bounce (reverse direction).
\end{enumerate}
\end{insightbox}

%=================================================================
% SECTION 9.4: CONSERVATION OF MOMENTUM
%=================================================================
\newpage
\section{Conservation of Momentum}

\subsection{What Is Conservation of Momentum?}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{law of conservation of momentum} states that if the net external force on a system is zero, the total momentum of the system remains constant:
\[
\vec{p}_{\text{total, initial}} = \vec{p}_{\text{total, final}}
\]
For a two-object system:
\[
m_1 \vec{v}_{1i} + m_2 \vec{v}_{2i} = m_1 \vec{v}_{1f} + m_2 \vec{v}_{2f}
\]
\end{definition}

This is one of the most powerful principles in all of physics. It applies to \emph{every} interaction---collisions, explosions, rocket propulsion, nuclear reactions---as long as external forces are negligible (or we focus on the brief moment of interaction when internal forces dominate).

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you and a friend are standing on a frozen pond (frictionless ice). You're holding a heavy medicine ball between you. When you push the ball to your friend, you slide backward. The total ``movement'' (momentum) of the you-plus-ball system stays the same: before the push it was zero, and after the push your backward momentum exactly cancels the ball's forward momentum. Momentum wasn't created or destroyed---it was just transferred.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Why Momentum Is Conserved: Newton's Third Law}

The conservation of momentum is a direct consequence of Newton's Third Law. Consider two objects that interact (collide, push, pull):

\begin{itemize}
\item Object 1 exerts force $\vec{F}_{1 \to 2}$ on Object 2.
\item Object 2 exerts force $\vec{F}_{2 \to 1} = -\vec{F}_{1 \to 2}$ on Object 1 (Newton's Third Law).
\item Both forces act for the same time interval $\Delta t$.
\end{itemize}

Therefore:
\[
\vec{J}_{\text{on 1}} = \vec{F}_{2 \to 1}\,\Delta t = -\vec{F}_{1 \to 2}\,\Delta t = -\vec{J}_{\text{on 2}}
\]

The impulse on Object 1 is equal and opposite to the impulse on Object 2. Since impulse equals change in momentum:
\[
\Delta\vec{p}_1 = -\Delta\vec{p}_2 \implies \Delta\vec{p}_1 + \Delta\vec{p}_2 = 0
\]

The total momentum of the system does not change!

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Conservation of Momentum:} For an isolated system (no net external force), the total momentum before any interaction equals the total momentum after:
\[
\sum \vec{p}_{\text{before}} = \sum \vec{p}_{\text{after}}
\]
This holds regardless of the type of interaction (elastic, inelastic, explosion) and regardless of the internal forces involved.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Conditions for Conservation of Momentum}

\begin{criticalbox}
Momentum is conserved \textbf{only when the net external force on the system is zero} (or negligibly small compared to internal forces during the interaction). Common situations:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Collisions:} Internal collision forces are huge and brief; external forces (gravity, friction) are negligible during the short collision time.
\item \textbf{Explosions:} Internal explosive forces dominate; external forces are negligible during the brief explosion.
\item \textbf{Frictionless surfaces:} No external horizontal forces.
\item \textbf{Recoil:} Gun firing a bullet, person throwing an object on ice.
\end{itemize}

Momentum is \textbf{NOT} conserved when significant external forces act on the system over the time interval of interest (e.g., a ball falling under gravity over several seconds---gravity is an external force that changes the ball's momentum).
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{Types of Systems}

\begin{definition}
An \textbf{isolated system} is one in which no net external force acts. Only internal forces (between objects within the system) are present. Total momentum is conserved in an isolated system.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{non-isolated system} has external forces acting on it. Total momentum is \emph{not} conserved unless we account for the impulse from external forces:
\[
\vec{J}_{\text{external}} = \Delta\vec{p}_{\text{system}}
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Mistake: Confusing ``system'' boundaries.} If you include only one object in your system, the force from the other object is \emph{external}, and momentum of that single object is not conserved. You must include \
emph{all} interacting objects in your system for momentum to be conserved.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Applying Conservation of Momentum}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Problem-Solving Strategy for Conservation of Momentum:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Define the system:} Include all objects involved in the interaction.
\item \textbf{Verify isolation:} Confirm that external forces are zero or negligible during the interaction.
\item \textbf{Choose a coordinate system:} Define positive direction(s).
\item \textbf{Write the conservation equation:}
\[
m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = m_1 v_{1f} + m_2 v_{2f}
\]
\item \textbf{Substitute known values} and solve for the unknown(s).
\item \textbf{Check your answer:} Does the direction make sense? Are the magnitudes reasonable?
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 1: Recoil of a Cannon}

A 500\,kg cannon is mounted on a frictionless surface. It fires a 5\,kg cannonball at 100\,m/s. What is the recoil velocity of the cannon?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{System:} Cannon + cannonball.

\textbf{Isolation:} Frictionless surface, so no net external horizontal force.

\textbf{Before firing:} Both at rest, so $p_{\text{total},i} = 0$.

\textbf{After firing:} Let positive = direction of cannonball.
\[
0 = m_{\text{ball}} v_{\text{ball}} + m_{\text{cannon}} v_{\text{cannon}}
\]
\[
0 = (5)(100) + (500)(v_{\text{cannon}})
\]
\[
v_{\text{cannon}} = \frac{-500}{500} = -1.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

The cannon recoils at 1.0\,m/s in the direction opposite to the cannonball. The negative sign confirms the opposite direction.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 2: Two Skaters Pushing Apart}

Two ice skaters face each other at rest. Skater A (mass 50\,kg) pushes off Skater B (mass 75\,kg). After the push, Skater A moves backward at 3\,m/s. Find Skater B's velocity.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{System:} Both skaters. \textbf{Isolation:} Frictionless ice, no external horizontal forces.

Let positive = Skater B's direction of motion.
\[
p_i = 0 \quad (\text{both at rest})
\]
\[
0 = m_A v_A + m_B v_B = (50)(-3) + (75)(v_B)
\]
\[
v_B = \frac{150}{75} = +2.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

Skater B moves at 2.0\,m/s in the positive direction. Note that the lighter skater moves faster---this always happens when two objects push apart from rest.
\end{solution}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Adding Conservation of Momentum to Your Toolbox:}

You now have multiple tools for analyzing motion:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Definition of velocity:} $\vec{v} = \Delta\vec{r}/\Delta t$ (relates position and time)
\item \textbf{Definition of acceleration:} $\vec{a} = \Delta\vec{v}/\Delta t$ (relates velocity and time)
\item \textbf{Newton's Second Law / Forces:} $\vec{F}_{\text{net}} = m\vec{a}$ (relates force to acceleration)
\item \textbf{Conservation of Energy:} $K_i + U_i + W_{\text{nc}} = K_f + U_f$ (relates speeds at different positions)
\item \textbf{Conservation of Momentum:} $\vec{p}_i = \vec{p}_f$ (relates velocities before and after interactions)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{When to use momentum:} Whenever objects interact (collide, explode, push apart) and you want to relate velocities before and after the interaction. Momentum is especially powerful when forces are unknown or complicated (like collision forces).
\end{insightbox}

\begin{analogy}
Conservation of momentum is like a bank account shared by two people. If one person withdraws \$50, the other person's balance must increase by \$50---the total stays the same. In physics, if one object gains momentum in one direction, the other must gain equal momentum in the opposite direction, keeping the total unchanged.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Momentum Conservation with External Forces}

When external forces \emph{are} present but the collision time is very short, we can often still use conservation of momentum during the collision itself. The key insight is:

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Impulse Approximation:} During a very brief collision ($\Delta t \to 0$), the impulse from external forces ($\vec{F}_{\text{ext}} \Delta t$) becomes negligibly small compared to the large internal collision forces. Therefore, momentum is approximately conserved \emph{during} the collision, even if external forces exist.

After the collision, you must account for external forces using other methods (forces, energy, etc.).
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Practice Questions}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.4.1:} Under what condition is the total momentum of a system conserved?

\textbf{(A)} When no forces act on any object in the system \\
\textbf{(B)} When no net external force acts on the system \\
\textbf{(C)} When kinetic energy is conserved \\
\textbf{(D)} When all collisions are perfectly elastic

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Internal forces always cancel in pairs (Newton's third law), so they cannot change total momentum. Only a net \emph{external} force can change the total momentum of a system. When $\vec{F}_{\text{ext,net}} = 0$, momentum is conserved regardless of whether the collision is elastic or inelastic.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.4.2:} A 3\,kg cart moving at 4\,m/s to the right collides with a 1\,kg cart at rest. After the collision, the 3\,kg cart moves at 2\,m/s to the right. What is the velocity of the 1\,kg cart?

\textbf{(A)} 2\,m/s right \quad \textbf{(B)} 4\,m/s right \quad \textbf{(C)} 6\,m/s right \quad \textbf{(D)} 8\,m/s right

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = m_1 v_{1f} + m_2 v_{2f}
\]
\[
(3)(4) + (1)(0) = (3)(2) + (1)(v_{2f})
\]
\[
12 = 6 + v_{2f} \implies v_{2f} = 6\;\text{m/s to the right}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.4.3:} A 70\,kg astronaut floating at rest in space throws a 2\,kg wrench at 10\,m/s. What is the astronaut's recoil speed?

\textbf{(A)} 0.14\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 0.29\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 1.4\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 5.0\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
0 = (2)(10) + (70)(v_{\text{astro}})
\]
\[
v_{\text{astro}} = \frac{-20}{70} = -0.286\;\text{m/s} \approx 0.29\;\text{m/s (opposite direction)}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.4.4:} Two identical 5\,kg balls approach each other, each traveling at 3\,m/s. What is the total momentum of the system?

\textbf{(A)} 30\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 15\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 0\,kg$\cdot$m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} $-$15\,kg$\cdot$m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Let positive = right. One ball has $p = (5)(+3) = +15$\,kg$\cdot$m/s, the other has $p = (5)(-3) = -15$\,kg$\cdot$m/s. Total: $+15 + (-15) = 0$. Since total momentum is zero, after the collision the total must still be zero.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.4.5:} A 10\,kg object moving at 6\,m/s explodes into two pieces. A 4\,kg piece moves at 12\,m/s in the original direction. What is the velocity of the 6\,kg piece?

\textbf{(A)} 2\,m/s forward \quad \textbf{(B)} 2\,m/s backward \quad \textbf{(C)} 4\,m/s forward \quad \textbf{(D)} 0\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (A)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
(10)(6) = (4)(12) + (6)(v)
\]
\[
60 = 48 + 6v \implies v = \frac{12}{6} = 2\;\text{m/s forward}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.4.6:} Which of the following is an example of a system where momentum is NOT conserved?

\textbf{(A)} Two billiard balls colliding on a frictionless table \\
\textbf{(B)} A bullet embedding in a block on a frictionless surface \\
\textbf{(C)} A ball falling freely under gravity (system = ball only) \\
\textbf{(D)} Two skaters pushing apart on frictionless ice

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} For a ball falling under gravity, the gravitational force is an \emph{external} force on the ball (the Earth is not included in the system). This external force changes the ball's momentum. In all other options, the system is isolated (no net external force in the relevant direction).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Scenario: Rocket Propulsion via Momentum Conservation}

A model rocket (total mass 0.8\,kg including fuel) is at rest on a frictionless horizontal track. It ejects 0.1\,kg of exhaust gas at 50\,m/s to the left.

\textbf{Part A:} What is the velocity of the rocket immediately after this first fuel ejection?

\textbf{Part B:} Now the rocket (mass 0.7\,kg, moving from Part A) ejects another 0.1\,kg of gas at 50\,m/s \emph{relative to the ground} to the left. Find the rocket's new velocity.

\textbf{Part C:} Explain why the rocket gains more speed with each successive fuel ejection, even though the same mass of fuel is ejected at the same speed each time.

\tcblower
\textbf{Part A:}
\[
0 = (0.1)(-50) + (0.7)(v_{\text{rocket}})
\]
\[
v_{\text{rocket}} = \frac{5}{0.7} = 7.14\;\text{m/s to the right}
\]

\textbf{Part B:}
\[
(0.7)(7.14) = (0.1)(-50) + (0.6)(v_f)
\]
\[
5.0 = -5.0 + 0.6\,v_f \implies v_f = \frac{10.0}{0.6} = 16.67\;\text{m/s to the right}
\]

\textbf{Part C:} Each ejection provides the same impulse to the rocket, but the rocket's mass decreases with each ejection. Since $\Delta v = J/m$, a smaller mass results in a larger velocity change for the same impulse. This is the fundamental principle behind rocket propulsion---as fuel is burned and ejected, the remaining rocket becomes lighter and accelerates more efficiently.
\end{exercise}

%========================================================================
% SECTION 9.5: INELASTIC COLLISIONS
%========================================================================
\section{Inelastic Collisions}

\subsection{What Are Inelastic Collisions?}

\begin{definition}
An \textbf{inelastic collision} is a collision in which the total kinetic energy of the system is \emph{not} conserved. Some kinetic energy is converted into other forms (thermal energy, sound, deformation). However, \textbf{total momentum is still conserved} (assuming no net external force).
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{perfectly inel
astic collision} (also called a \textbf{perfectly inelastic collision}) is a special case of inelastic collision in which the two objects \emph{stick together} after the collision and move as one combined object. This type of collision loses the \emph{maximum} amount of kinetic energy (while still conserving momentum).
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine two lumps of clay thrown at each other. When they collide, they smush together into one big lump and move off together. That's a perfectly inelastic collision. Compare this to two billiard balls that bounce off each other---that's closer to an elastic collision. The clay ``wastes'' energy deforming, while the billiard balls bounce back with most of their energy intact.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Classification of Collisions}

\begin{center}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{3.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{3cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Property} & \textbf{Perfectly Inelastic} & \textbf{Inelastic} & \textbf{Elastic} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Property} & \textbf{Perfectly Inelastic} & \textbf{Inelastic} & \textbf{Elastic} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Objects after collision & Stick together & Separate & Separate \\
\midrule
Momentum conserved? & Yes & Yes & Yes \\
\midrule
Kinetic energy conserved? & No (maximum loss) & No (some loss) & Yes (no loss) \\
\midrule
Total energy conserved? & Yes (energy transforms) & Yes (energy transforms) & Yes \\
\midrule
Real-world examples & Car crash where cars lock together, catching a ball & Most real collisions (cars that bounce apart, sports impacts) & Atomic/molecular collisions, nearly ideal billiard balls \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Perfectly Inelastic Collision Equations}

For two objects that stick together:

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Perfectly Inelastic Collision:}
\[
m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = (m_1 + m_2)\,v_f
\]
Solving for the final velocity:
\[
v_f = \frac{m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i}}{m_1 + m_2}
\]
This is simply the conservation of momentum equation with the constraint that both objects share the same final velocity $v_f$.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Energy Lost in Perfectly Inelastic Collisions}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding Energy Lost in a Perfectly Inelastic Collision:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Find $v_f$} using conservation of momentum.
\item \textbf{Calculate initial kinetic energy:}
\[
K_i = \frac{1}{2}m_1 v_{1i}^2 + \frac{1}{2}m_2 v_{2i}^2
\]
\item \textbf{Calculate final kinetic energy:}
\[
K_f = \frac{1}{2}(m_1 + m_2)\,v_f^2
\]
\item \textbf{Energy lost:}
\[
\Delta K = K_f - K_i \quad (\text{this will be negative})
\]
\item The lost kinetic energy has been converted to thermal energy, sound, and deformation.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Fraction of Energy Lost:} For a perfectly inelastic collision where object 2 is initially at rest:
\[
\frac{|\Delta K|}{K_i} = \frac{m_2}{m_1 + m_2}
\]
This means:
\begin{itemize}
\item If a heavy object hits a light one, most energy is retained.
\item If a light object hits a heavy one, most energy is lost.
\item If equal masses collide, exactly half the kinetic energy is lost.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{General Inelastic Collisions}

In a general inelastic collision, the objects do \emph{not} stick together. They separate after the collision, but kinetic energy is still not conserved. In this case, you have:
\[
m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = m_1 v_{1f} + m_2 v_{2f}
\]
This is one equation with two unknowns ($v_{1f}$ and $v_{2f}$), so you need additional information (e.g., one of the final velocities, or the fraction of energy lost) to solve the problem.

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Key Distinction:} In a perfectly inelastic collision, the constraint ``they stick together'' ($v_{1f} = v_{2f} = v_f$) reduces the unknowns to one, making the problem solvable with momentum conservation alone. In a general inelastic collision, you need more information beyond just momentum conservation.
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{Elastic Collisions (Brief Comparison)}

\begin{definition}
An \textbf{elastic collision} is one in which \emph{both} momentum and kinetic energy are conserved. In an elastic collision between two objects:
\[
m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = m_1 v_{1f} + m_2 v_{2f} \quad \text{(momentum)}
\]
\[
\frac{1}{2}m_1 v_{1i}^2 + \frac{1}{2}m_2 v_{2i}^2 = \frac{1}{2}m_1 v_{1f}^2 + \frac{1}{2}m_2 v_{2f}^2 \quad \text{(kinetic energy)}
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Elastic Collision with Target at Rest:} When object 2 is initially at rest ($v_{2i} = 0$), the solutions are:
\[
v_{1f} = \frac{m_1 - m_2}{m_1 + m_2}\,v_{1i}, \qquad v_{2f} = \frac{2m_1}{m_1 + m_2}\,v_{1i}
\]
Special cases:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Equal masses} ($m_1 = m_2$): $v_{1f} = 0$, $v_{2f} = v_{1i}$ (complete transfer).
\item \textbf{Heavy projectile} ($m_1 \gg m_2$): $v_{1f} \approx v_{1i}$, $v_{2f} \approx 2v_{1i}$ (light target flies off fast).
\item \textbf{Light projectile} ($m_1 \ll m_2$): $v_{1f} \approx -v_{1i}$, $v_{2f} \approx 0$ (projectile bounces back).
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Explosions as ``Reverse'' Collisions}

\begin{definition}
An \textbf{explosion} is the reverse of a perfectly inelastic collision: a single object breaks apart into two or more pieces. If the system is isolated, momentum is conserved:
\[
\vec{p}_{\text{before}} = \vec{p}_{\text{after}}
\]
If the object was initially at rest, the total momentum after the explosion is zero:
\[
0 = m_1\vec{v}_1 + m_2\vec{v}_2 \implies m_1\vec{v}_1 = -m_2\vec{v}_2
\]
The pieces fly off in opposite directions with momenta of equal magnitude.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Think of two ice skaters standing face-to-face on a frictionless ice rink. They push off each other and slide in opposite directions. The heavier skater moves slower, the lighter skater moves faster, but their momenta are equal and opposite. Together, the total momentum is still zero---just as it was before they pushed.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Worked Examples}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 1: Perfectly Inelastic Collision}

A 1200\,kg car traveling east at 15\,m/s collides with a 800\,kg car traveling west at 10\,m/s. They lock bumpers and move together. Find (a) the final velocity and (b) the kinetic energy lost.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{(a)} Define east as positive.
\[
m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = (m_1 + m_2)\,v_f
\]
\[
(1200)(15) + (800)(-10) = (1200 + 800)\,v_f
\]
\[
18{,}000 - 8{,}000 = 2000\,v_f
\]
\[
v_f = \frac{10{,}000}{2000} = 5\;\text{m/s (east)}
\]

\textbf{(b)} Initial kinetic energy:
\[
K_i = \frac{1}{2}(1200)(15)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(800)(10)^2 = 135{,}000 + 40{,}000 = 175{,}000\;\text{J}
\]
Final kinetic energy:
\[
K_f = \frac{1}{2}(2000)(5)^2 = 25{,}000\;\text{J}
\]
Energy lost:
\[
|\Delta K| = 175{,}000 - 25{,}000 = 150{,}000\;\text{J} = 150\;\text{kJ}
\]
About 85.7\% of the kinetic energy was converted to thermal energy, sound, and deformation.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 2: Ballistic Pendulum}

A 10\,g bullet is fired horizontally into a 2.0\,kg wooden block suspended from strings (a ballistic pendulum). The bullet embeds in the block, and the block+bullet combination swings upward to a height of 0.45\,m. Find the speed of the bullet just before impact.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
This is a two-stage problem combining momentum conservation and energy conservation.

\textbf{Stage 1: Collision (momentum conservation)}

The bullet embeds in the block---perfectly inelastic collision:
\[
m_b v_b = (m_b + m_B)\,v_f
\]
where $m_b = 0.010$\,kg, $m_B = 2.0$\,kg.

\textbf{Stage 2: Swing upward (energy conservation)}

After the collision, the block+bullet swings upward. No external work is done (tension is perpendicular to motion), so:
\[
\frac{1}{2}(m_b + m_B)v_f^2 = (m_b + m_B)g\,h
\]
\[
v_f = \sqrt{2gh} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(0.45)} = \sqrt{8.82} = 2.97\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Back to Stage 1:}
\[
v_b = \frac{(m_b + m_B)\,v_f}{m_b} = \frac{(2.010)(2.97)}{0.010} = \frac{5.97}{0.010} = 597\;\text{m/s}
\]

The bullet was traveling at approximately 597\,m/s.
\end{solution}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{The Ballistic Pendulum Trick:} You \emph{cannot} use energy conservation across the collision (energy is lost!). You \emph{cannot} use momentum conservation during the swing (gravity is an external force). The key is to use the right principle at the right stage:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{During collision:} Momentum is conserved (forces are internal and brief).
\item \textbf{During swing:} Energy is conserved (no non-conservative work).
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 3: Explosion---Recoil of a Cannon}

A 500\,kg cannon is initially at rest. It fires a 5\,kg cannonball at 100\,m/s horizontally. Find the recoil velocity of the cannon.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The system (cannon + cannonball) is initially at rest, so $p_i = 0$.

By conservation of momentum:
\[
0 = m_{\text{ball}}\,v_{\text{ball}} + m_{\text{cannon}}\,v_{\text{cannon}}
\]
\[
v_{\text{cannon}} = -\frac{m_{\text{ball}}\,v_{\text{ball}}}{m_{\text{cannon}}} = -\frac{(5)(100)}{500} = -1\;\text{m/s}
\]

The cannon recoils at 1\,m/s in the direction opposite to the cannonball.
\end{solution}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Mistake: Using energy conservation for inelastic collisions.} In any inelastic collision (including perfectly inelastic), kinetic energy is NOT conserved. You cannot set $K_i = K_f$. Only use $K_i = K_f$ for elastic collisions. Always use momentum conservation as your primary tool for collisions.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Practice Questions}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.1:} A 5\,kg cart moving at 4\,m/s collides with and sticks to a 3\,kg cart at rest. What is the final speed of the combined carts?

\textbf{(A)} 1.25\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 2.5\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 4.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 7.0\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
v_f = \frac{m_1 v_{1i}}{m_1 + m_2} = \frac{(5)(4)}{5+3} = \frac{20}{8} = 2.5\;\text{m/s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.2:} In which type of collision is kinetic energy conserved?

\textbf{(A)} Perfectly inelastic \quad \textbf{(B)} Inelastic \quad \textbf{(C)} Elastic \quad \textbf{(D)} Explosive

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} By definition, elastic collisions conserve both momentum and kinetic energy. Inelastic and perfectly inelastic collisions conserve momentum but not kinetic energy. Explosions increase kinetic energy (converting internal energy to kinetic energy).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.3:} A 2\,kg object moving at 6\,m/s collides elastically with a 2\,kg object at rest. What are the final velocities?

\textbf{(A)} Both move at 3\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} First stops, second moves at 6\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} First moves at 6\,m/s, second stays at rest \quad \textbf{(D)} Both move at 6\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} For equal-mass elastic collisions with the target at rest, there is a complete transfer of motion: $v_{1f} = 0$ and $v_{2f} = v_{1i} = 6$
\,m/s. This is the ``Newton's cradle'' effect---in a head-on elastic collision between equal masses, the velocities simply exchange.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.4:} A 10\,kg object moving east at 3\,m/s collides with and sticks to a 5\,kg object moving west at 6\,m/s. What is the velocity of the combined object after the collision?

\textbf{(A)} 0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 1\,m/s east \quad \textbf{(C)} 1\,m/s west \quad \textbf{(D)} 2\,m/s east

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (A)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Let east be positive.
\[
p_i = (10)(3) + (5)(-6) = 30 - 30 = 0
\]
\[
p_f = (10 + 5)v_f = 0 \implies v_f = 0\;\text{m/s}
\]
The combined object is at rest after the collision.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.5:} A perfectly inelastic collision always results in:

\textbf{(A)} Maximum kinetic energy after the collision \\
\textbf{(B)} The objects bouncing apart \\
\textbf{(C)} The objects sticking together \\
\textbf{(D)} Conservation of kinetic energy

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} By definition, a perfectly inelastic collision is one in which the objects stick together. This results in the maximum loss of kinetic energy (not conservation of it).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.6:} A bullet of mass 0.01\,kg traveling at 400\,m/s embeds in a 2\,kg wooden block at rest on a frictionless surface. What is the speed of the block-bullet system after impact?

\textbf{(A)} 0.5\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 1.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 1.99\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 200\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
v_f = \frac{m_{\text{bullet}} v_{\text{bullet}}}{m_{\text{bullet}} + m_{\text{block}}} = \frac{(0.01)(400)}{0.01 + 2} = \frac{4}{2.01} \approx 1.99\;\text{m/s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.5.7:} What fraction of the kinetic energy is lost in the bullet-block collision from Question 9.5.6?

\textbf{(A)} About 0.5\% \quad \textbf{(B)} About 50\% \quad \textbf{(C)} About 95\% \quad \textbf{(D)} About 99.5\%

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (D)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
K_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.01)(400)^2 = 800\;\text{J}
\]
\[
K_f = \frac{1}{2}(2.01)(1.99)^2 \approx 3.98\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\text{Fraction lost} = \frac{800 - 3.98}{800} \approx 0.995 = 99.5\%
\]
Nearly all kinetic energy is converted to thermal energy and deformation. This dramatically illustrates how perfectly inelastic collisions can waste almost all kinetic energy while still conserving momentum.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Scenario: Ballistic Pendulum}

A classic physics experiment uses a ballistic pendulum to measure bullet speed. A bullet of mass $m = 0.012$\,kg is fired into a wooden block of mass $M = 3.0$\,kg suspended by strings. The bullet embeds in the block, and the block-bullet combination swings upward to a maximum height $h = 0.08$\,m.

\textbf{Part A:} Use conservation of momentum to find the velocity of the block-bullet system immediately after the collision in terms of the bullet's initial speed $v_0$.

\textbf{Part B:} Use conservation of energy (after the collision, as the block swings upward) to find the velocity of the block-bullet system just after the collision.

\textbf{Part C:} Combine Parts A and B to determine the bullet's initial speed $v_0$.

\textbf{Part D:} Calculate the fraction of kinetic energy lost in the collision.

\tcblower
\textbf{Part A:} Conservation of momentum (perfectly inelastic collision):
\[
m v_0 = (m + M)V \implies V = \frac{m v_0}{m + M} = \frac{0.012\,v_0}{3.012}
\]

\textbf{Part B:} After the collision, the block-bullet swings upward. Using conservation of energy (no external work after collision):
\[
\frac{1}{2}(m+M)V^2 = (m+M)g h
\]
\[
V = \sqrt{2gh} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(0.08)} = \sqrt{1.568} = 1.252\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Part C:} Setting the two expressions for $V$ equal:
\[
\frac{0.012\,v_0}{3.012} = 1.252 \implies v_0 = \frac{(1.252)(3.012)}{0.012} = 314\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Part D:}
\[
K_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.012)(314)^2 = 591\;\text{J}
\]
\[
K_f = \frac{1}{2}(3.012)(1.252)^2 = 2.36\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\text{Fraction lost} = \frac{591 - 2.36}{591} = 0.996 = 99.6\%
\]

This is a beautiful example of using \emph{both} conservation laws strategically: momentum conservation during the collision (when energy is not conserved), and energy conservation after the collision (when momentum is not conserved due to the string tension and gravity acting as external forces on the pendulum).
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary of Section 9.5}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways---Inelastic Collisions:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Momentum is conserved in ALL collisions (elastic, inelastic, perfectly inelastic).
\item Kinetic energy is conserved ONLY in elastic collisions.
\item In perfectly inelastic collisions, objects stick together: $v_f = \frac{m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i}}{m_1 + m_2}$.
\item The fraction of KE lost in a perfectly inelastic collision depends on the mass ratio.
\item Explosions are the reverse of perfectly inelastic collisions: one object becomes two, and internal energy is converted to kinetic energy.
\item The ballistic pendulum is a classic application combining momentum conservation (collision) with energy conservation (swing).
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%========================================================================
% SECTION 9.6: MOMENTUM AND COLLISIONS IN TWO DIMENSIONS
%========================================================================
\section{Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions}

\subsection{What Are Two-Dimensional Collisions?}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{two-dimensional collision} is a collision in which the objects' velocities are not all along a single line. After the collision, objects may move in different directions in a plane. Conservation of momentum must be applied \emph{independently} in each coordinate direction.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Think of a game of pool (billiards). When the cue ball hits another ball at an angle, the two balls fly off in different directions---not just forward and backward along one line. To figure out where each ball goes, you have to track the sideways momentum and the forward momentum separately, like keeping track of how far north \emph{and} how far east someone walks.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Conservation of Momentum in Two Dimensions}

Since momentum is a vector, conservation of momentum in two dimensions means:

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Conservation of Momentum in 2D:} For an isolated system, the total momentum is conserved in \emph{each} component direction independently:

\textbf{$x$-direction:}
\[
m_1 v_{1ix} + m_2 v_{2ix} = m_1 v_{1fx} + m_2 v_{2fx}
\]

\textbf{$y$-direction:}
\[
m_1 v_{1iy} + m_2 v_{2iy} = m_1 v_{1fy} + m_2 v_{2fy}
\]

These are two independent equations, which means you can solve for up to two unknowns.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Decomposing Velocities into Components}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Strategy for 2D Collision Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Draw a diagram:} Sketch the before and after situations, showing velocity vectors and angles.
\item \textbf{Choose a coordinate system:} Typically align the $x$-axis with the initial direction of motion of one object.
\item \textbf{Decompose all velocities into $x$ and $y$ components:}
\[
v_x = v\cos\theta, \qquad v_y = v\sin\theta
\]
\item \textbf{Apply conservation of momentum separately} in the $x$ and $y$ directions.
\item \textbf{If elastic:} Also apply conservation of kinetic energy for a third equation.
\item \textbf{Solve the system of equations} for the unknowns.
\item \textbf{Reconstruct final velocities:} Find magnitude and direction:
\[
v = \sqrt{v_x^2 + v_y^2}, \qquad \theta = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{v_y}{v_x}\right)
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\subsection{Worked Examples}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 1: Perfectly Inelastic 2D Collision}

A 1500\,kg car traveling east at 20\,m/s collides with a 2000\,kg truck traveling north at 15\,m/s at an intersection. The vehicles lock together. Find the speed and direction of the wreckage immediately after the collision.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Step 1--2:} Let east = $+x$, north = $+y$.

\textbf{Step 3:} Before collision:
\begin{itemize}
\item Car: $v_{1ix} = 20$\,m/s, $v_{1iy} = 0$
\item Truck: $v_{2ix} = 0$, $v_{2iy} = 15$\,m/s
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 4:} Conservation of momentum ($x$-direction):
\[
(1500)(20) + (2000)(0) = (1500 + 2000)v_{fx}
\]
\[
30{,}000 = 3500\,v_{fx} \implies v_{fx} = 8.57\;\text{m/s}
\]

Conservation of momentum ($y$-direction):
\[
(1500)(0) + (2000)(15) = 3500\,v_{fy}
\]
\[
30{,}000 = 3500\,v_{fy} \implies v_{fy} = 8.57\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Step 7:} Final speed:
\[
v_f = \sqrt{v_{fx}^2 + v_{fy}^2} = \sqrt{8.57^2 + 8.57^2} = \sqrt{73.4 + 73.4} = \sqrt{146.9} = 12.1\;\text{m/s}
\]

Direction:
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\!\left(\frac{8.57}{8.57}\right) = \tan^{-1}(1) = 45^\circ \text{ north of east}
\]

The wreckage moves at 12.1\,m/s at $45^\circ$ north of east.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 2: Glancing Elastic Collision}

A 0.25\,kg puck moving at 6\,m/s in the $+x$ direction strikes a stationary 0.25\,kg puck. After the collision, the first puck moves at $30^\circ$ above the $x$-axis. Find the speeds of both pucks and the direction of the second puck.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
For an elastic collision between equal masses where one is initially at rest, there is a special result: the two pucks move at $90^\circ$ to each other after the collision.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum ($x$):}
\[
(0.25)(6) = (0.25)v_{1f}\cos 30^\circ + (0.25)v_{2f}\cos\phi
\]
\[
6 = v_{1f}\cos 30^\circ + v_{2f}\cos\phi \quad \text{...(1)}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of momentum ($y$):}
\[
0 = (0.25)v_{1f}\sin 30^\circ - (0.25)v_{2f}\sin\phi
\]
\[
0 = v_{1f}\sin 30^\circ - v_{2f}\sin\phi \quad \text{...(2)}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of kinetic energy:}
\[
\frac{1}{2}(0.25)(6)^2 = \frac{1}{2}(0.25)v_{1f}^2 + \frac{1}{2}(0.25)v_{2f}^2
\]
\[
36 = v_{1f}^2 + v_{2f}^2 \quad \text{...(3)}
\]

Using the equal-mass elastic collision property: $\phi = 90^\circ - 30^\circ = 60^\circ$ below the $x$-axis.

From equation (2): $v_{1f}\sin 30^\circ = v_{2f}\sin 60^\circ$
\[
v_{1f}(0.5) = v_{2f}(0.866) \implies v_{1f} = 1.732\,v_{2f}
\]

Substituting into equation (3):
\[
(1.732\,v_{2f})^2 + v_{2f}^2 = 36 \implies 3v_{2f}^2 + v_{2f}^2 = 36 \implies 4v_{2f}^2 = 36
\]
\[
v_{2f} = 3\;\text{m/s}, \qquad v_{1f} = 1.732(3) = 5.20\;\text{m/s}
\]

The first puck moves at 5.20\,m/s at $30^\circ$ above the $x$-axis; the second puck moves at 3.0\,m/s at $60^\circ$ below the $x$-axis.
\end{solution}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Special Result for Equal-Mass Elastic Collisions:} When a moving object collides elastically with a stationary object of equal mass, the two objects always move at $90^\circ$ to each other after the collision. This is why billiard balls spread at right angles!
\end{insightbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 3: Explosion in Two Dimensions}

A 10\,kg firework at rest explodes into three pieces. Piece A (3\,kg) moves east at 20\,m/s. Piece B (4\,kg) moves north at 15\,m/s. Find the velocity (speed and direction) of Piece C (3\,kg).
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Total initial momentum is zero (at rest).
\textbf{Conservation of momentum ($x$):}
\[
0 = (3)(20) + 0 + (3)v_{Cx} \implies v_{Cx} = -20\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of momentum ($y$):}
\[
0 = 0 + (4)(15) + (3)v_{Cy} \implies v_{Cy} = -20\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Speed of Piece C:}
\[
v_C = \sqrt{(-20)^2 + (-20)^2} = \sqrt{800} = 28.3\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Direction of Piece C:}
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{|-20|}{|-20|}\right) = 45^\circ
\]
Since both components are negative, Piece C moves $45^\circ$ south of west (or $225^\circ$ measured counterclockwise from east).
\end{solution}

\subsection{The Center of Mass and 2D Collisions}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Center of Mass Insight:} In any collision or explosion with no external forces, the center of mass of the system continues to move with the same velocity throughout the interaction. In the firework example above, the center of mass was at rest before the explosion and remains at rest after---the momentum of all three pieces sums to zero.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Comparison: 1D vs.\ 2D Collision Problems}

\begin{center}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{3.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{1D Collisions} & \textbf{2D Collisions} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{1D Collisions} & \textbf{2D Collisions} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\bottomrule
\endfoot
Momentum equations & 1 equation (along line of motion) & 2 equations ($x$ and $y$ independently) \\
\midrule
Directions & Along a single axis; use $+/-$ signs & Must resolve vectors into components \\
\midrule
Angles & Not needed & Angles measured from reference axis \\
\midrule
Energy equation & 1 scalar equation (if elastic) & 1 scalar equation (if elastic) \\
\midrule
Unknowns typically & 1--2 unknowns & 2--4 unknowns (speeds and angles) \\
\midrule
Difficulty & Algebraic & Trigonometric + algebraic \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Practice Questions}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.6.1:} A 2\,kg ball moving east at 5\,m/s strikes a stationary 2\,kg ball. After the collision, the first ball moves at $30^\circ$ north of east at 4\,m/s. What is the $x$-component of the second ball's velocity?

\textbf{(A)} 1.54\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 2.00\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 3.46\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 5.00\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (A)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
p_{xi} = (2)(5) = 10\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]
\[
p_{xf} = (2)(4\cos 30^\circ) + (2)(v_{2x})
\]
\[
10 = 6.928 + 2v_{2x} \implies v_{2x} = \frac{3.072}{2} = 1.54\;\text{m/s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.6.2:} In a 2D collision, which of the following is conserved?

\textbf{(A)} Only the $x$-component of momentum \\
\textbf{(B)} Only the $y$-component of momentum \\
\textbf{(C)} Both the $x$- and $y$-components of momentum independently \\
\textbf{(D)} Only total kinetic energy

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Momentum is a vector quantity. Conservation of momentum means each component is independently conserved: $\sum p_x$ is constant AND $\sum p_y$ is constant. Kinetic energy is only conserved in elastic collisions.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.6.3:} A 5\,kg object at rest explodes into two pieces. Piece 1 (2\,kg) moves at 10\,m/s at $90^\circ$ (north). What is the speed of Piece 2 (3\,kg)?

\textbf{(A)} 5.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 6.67\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 10.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 15.0\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Initial momentum = 0. So:
\[
0 = (2)(10) + (3)(v_2) \implies v_2 = -\frac{20}{3} = -6.67\;\text{m/s}
\]
Piece 2 moves south at 6.67\,m/s.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.6.4:} Two equal-mass pucks undergo an elastic collision on a frictionless surface. One is initially at rest. After the collision, the angle between their velocity vectors is:

\textbf{(A)} $0^\circ$ \quad \textbf{(B)} $45^\circ$ \quad \textbf{(C)} $60^\circ$ \quad \textbf{(D)} $90^\circ$

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (D)}

\textbf{Rationale:} For elastic collisions between equal masses (one initially at rest), the two objects always separate at $90^\circ$ to each other. This can be proven by combining conservation of momentum (vector equation) with conservation of kinetic energy (scalar equation).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.6.5:} A 4\,kg object moving at 3\,m/s in the $+x$ direction collides with a 4\,kg object moving at 3\,m/s in the $+y$ direction. They stick together. What is the speed of the combined object?

\textbf{(A)} 0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 1.5\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 2.12\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 3.0\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
p_x = (4)(3) = 12\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}, \quad p_y = (4)(3) = 12\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]
\[
v_f = \frac{\sqrt{p_x^2 + p_y^2}}{m_{\text{total}}} = \frac{\sqrt{144 + 144}}{8} = \frac{16.97}{8} = 2.12\;\text{m/s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 9.6.6:} A bomb at rest explodes into three equal pieces (each 2\,kg). Two pieces fly off: one at 10\,m/s east and one at 10\,m/s north. What is the speed of the third piece?

\textbf{(A)} 10\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 14.1\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 20\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 7.07\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:}
\[
v_{3x} = -\frac{(2)(10)}{2} = -10\;\text{m/s}, \quad v_{3y} = -\frac{(2)(10)}{2} = -10\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
v_3 = \sqrt{(-10)^2 + (-10)^2} = \sqrt{200} = 14.1\;\text{m/s}
\]
The third piece moves at $14.1$\,m/s in the southwest direction ($225^\circ$).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Scenario: Hockey Collision in 2D}

On a frictionless ice rink, Player A (80\,kg) skates east at 5\,m/s and Player B (100\,kg) skates north at 4\,m/s. They collide and grab onto each other.

\textbf{Part A:} Find the $x$- and $y$-components of the final velocity.

\textbf{Part B:} Find the speed and direction of the combined players.

\textbf{Part C:} Calculate the fraction of kinetic energy lost in the collision.

\textbf{Part D:} Where did the lost kinetic energy go?

\tcblower
\textbf{Part A:}
\[
v_{fx} = \frac{m_A v_{Ax}}{m_A + m_B} = \frac{(80)(5)}{180} = 2.22\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
v_{fy} = \frac{m_B v_{By}}{m_A + m_B} = \frac{(100)(4)}{180} = 2.22\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Part B:}
\[
v_f = \sqrt{2.22^2 + 2.22^2} = \sqrt{9.87} = 3.14\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{2.22}{2.22}\right) = 45^\circ \;\text{north of east}
\]
