\Delta x_{\text{before}} = 30.1 \times 4.07 = 122.5\;\text{m}
\]

Total range from launch point:
\[
R = 122.5 + 245.0 = 367.5\;\text{m}
\]

This problem beautifully integrates projectile motion, energy conservation, and conservation of momentum!
\end{exercise}

%========================================================================
% SECTION 3.7: CIRCULAR MOTION
%========================================================================

\section{Circular Motion}

\subsection{What Is Circular Motion?}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Uniform circular motion} is the motion of an object traveling at a \emph{constant speed} along a circular path. Although the speed is constant, the \emph{velocity} is continuously changing because the direction is continuously changing. This change in velocity means there must
be an acceleration directed toward the center of the circle.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you're on a merry-go-round spinning at a steady rate. You're moving at the same speed the whole time, but you're constantly changing direction---first you face north, then east, then south, then west. Because your direction keeps changing, your velocity keeps changing, even though your speedometer would read the same number. The fact that your velocity changes means something must be pushing you---that push is always directed toward the center of the merry-go-round (you can feel it through the pole you're holding). That inward push is what physicists call centripetal force, and the inward change in velocity is centripetal acceleration.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Key Quantities in Circular Motion}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Period ($T$)} is the time for one complete revolution around the circle. It is measured in seconds (s).
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Frequency ($f$)} is the number of complete revolutions per unit time. It is measured in hertz (Hz = rev/s). The relationship between period and frequency is:
\[
f = \frac{1}{T} \qquad \text{and} \qquad T = \frac{1}{f}
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Centripetal acceleration ($a_c$)} is the acceleration directed toward the center of the circular path. Its magnitude is:
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r}
\]
where $v$ is the speed and $r$ is the radius of the circle.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Centripetal force ($F_c$)} is the net force directed toward the center of the circular path that causes circular motion:
\[
F_c = m\,a_c = \frac{mv^2}{r}
\]
Centripetal force is \emph{not} a new type of force---it is the \emph{role} played by real forces (tension, gravity, friction, normal force) that happen to point toward the center.
\end{definition}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Centripetal force is NOT a separate force!} It is the name we give to whatever real force (or combination of forces) provides the inward acceleration. Never draw ``centripetal force'' as a separate arrow on a free-body diagram. Instead, identify which real force(s) point toward the center.

\textbf{Examples:}
\begin{itemize}
\item A ball on a string: tension provides centripetal force
\item A car turning on a flat road: static friction provides centripetal force
\item A satellite orbiting Earth: gravity provides centripetal force
\item A car on a banked curve: a component of the normal force provides centripetal force
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{There is no ``centrifugal force''!} In an inertial (non-accelerating) reference frame, there is no outward force on an object moving in a circle. The sensation of being ``pushed outward'' is simply your body's inertia---your body wants to go straight, but the car/ride/string forces you inward. Always analyze circular motion from the perspective of an inward (centripetal) net force.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Speed and Period Relationship}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Speed from Circumference and Period:} An object moving in a circle of radius $r$ with period $T$ travels one circumference per period:
\[
v = \frac{2\pi r}{T} = 2\pi r f
\]
Substituting into the centripetal acceleration formula:
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{4\pi^2 r}{T^2}
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 1: Centripetal Acceleration of a Spinning Wheel}

A bicycle wheel has a radius of 0.35\,m and completes 2 revolutions per second. Find: (a) the period, (b) the speed of a point on the rim, and (c) the centripetal acceleration of a point on the rim.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{(a)} $f = 2$\,Hz, so:
\[
T = \frac{1}{f} = \frac{1}{2} = 0.50\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{(b)} Speed:
\[
v = \frac{2\pi r}{T} = \frac{2\pi(0.35)}{0.50} = \frac{2.199}{0.50} = 4.40\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(c)} Centripetal acceleration:
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{(4.40)^2}{0.35} = \frac{19.36}{0.35} = 55.3\;\text{m/s}^2
\]

This is about 5.6 times the acceleration due to gravity! Points on spinning wheels experience very large accelerations.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 2: Force on a Car Rounding a Curve}

A 1200\,kg car rounds a flat curve of radius 50\,m at 15\,m/s. (a) What centripetal acceleration does the car experience? (b) What force provides this acceleration, and how large is it? (c) What is the minimum coefficient of static friction needed?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{(a)} Centripetal acceleration:
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{(15)^2}{50} = \frac{225}{50} = 4.50\;\text{m/s}^2
\]

\textbf{(b)} On a flat road, static friction provides the centripetal force:
\[
F_c = m\,a_c = (1200)(4.50) = 5400\;\text{N}
\]

\textbf{(c)} The friction force must equal the centripetal force:
\[
f_s = \mu_s\,N = \mu_s\,mg
\]
\[
\mu_s\,mg = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies \mu_s = \frac{v^2}{rg} = \frac{(15)^2}{(50)(9.8)} = \frac{225}{490} = 0.459
\]

If the road is wet and $\mu_s < 0.459$, the car will skid!
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 3: Satellite in Orbit (Using Gravity as Centripetal Force)}

The International Space Station orbits Earth at an altitude of about 408\,km above Earth's surface. Earth's radius is $R_E = 6.37 \times 10^6$\,m. If the gravitational acceleration at that altitude is approximately $8.67\;\text{m/s}^2$, find the orbital speed and period.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The orbital radius is:
\[
r = R_E + h = 6.37 \times 10^6 + 0.408 \times 10^6 = 6.778 \times 10^6\;\text{m}
\]

Gravity provides the centripetal acceleration:
\[
a_c = g_{\text{orbit}} = 8.67\;\text{m/s}^2
\]

From $a_c = v^2/r$:
\[
v = \sqrt{a_c \cdot r} = \sqrt{(8.67)(6.778 \times 10^6)} = \sqrt{5.877 \times 10^7} = 7667\;\text{m/s} \approx 7.67\;\text{km/s}
\]

Period:
\[
T = \frac{2\pi r}{v} = \frac{2\pi(6.778 \times 10^6)}{7667} = \frac{4.258 \times 10^7}{7667} = 5553\;\text{s} \approx 92.6\;\text{min}
\]

The ISS completes one orbit in about 93 minutes---roughly 15.5 orbits per day!
\end{solution}

\subsection{Vertical Circular Motion}

When an object moves in a vertical circle, the analysis is more complex because gravity acts differently at different points in the circle.

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Analyzing Vertical Circular Motion:}

\textbf{Step 1:} Draw a free-body diagram at the point of interest (top, bottom, or side).

\textbf{Step 2:} Identify which forces point toward the center and which point away.

\textbf{Step 3:} Apply Newton's second law in the radial direction (toward center = positive):
\[
\sum F_{\text{toward center}} = \frac{mv^2}{r}
\]

\textbf{At the bottom of the circle:}
\[
N - mg = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies N = mg + \frac{mv^2}{r}
\]
(Normal force must support weight AND provide centripetal force---you feel heavier.)

\textbf{At the top of the circle:}
\[
mg + N = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies N = \frac{mv^2}{r} - mg
\]
(Gravity helps provide centripetal force---you feel lighter.)

\textbf{Step 4:} For the minimum speed at the top (where $N = 0$ or tension $= 0$):
\[
mg = \frac{mv_{\min}^2}{r} \implies v_{\min} = \sqrt{gr}
\]
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 4: Ball on a String in a Vertical Circle}

A 0.50\,kg ball is swung in a vertical circle of radius 1.2\,m. At the top of the circle, the speed is 4.0\,m/s. Find: (a) the tension in the string at the top, and (b) the speed and tension at the bottom (using energy conservation).
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{(a) At the top:} Both gravity and tension point toward the center (downward):
\[
T_{\text{top}} + mg = \frac{mv_{\text{top}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
T_{\text{top}} = \frac{mv_{\text{top}}^2}{r} - mg = \frac{(0.50)(4.0)^2}{1.2} - (0.50)(9.8)
\]
\[
T_{\text{top}} = \frac{8.0}{1.2} - 4.9 = 6.67 - 4.9 = 1.77\;\text{N}
\]

\textbf{(b) Speed at the bottom using energy conservation:}

Taking the bottom as the reference level ($y = 0$), the top is at $y = 2r = 2.4$\,m:
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{top}}^2 + mg(2r) = \frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{bot}}^2 + 0
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot}} = \sqrt{v_{\text{top}}^2 + 4gr} = \sqrt{(4.0)^2 + 4(9.8)(1.2)} = \sqrt{16 + 47.04} = \sqrt{63.04} = 7.94\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Tension at the bottom:} At the bottom, tension points up (toward center) and gravity points down (away from center):
\[
T_{\text{bot}} - mg = \frac{mv_{\text{bot}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
T_{\text{bot}} = mg + \frac{mv_{\text{bot}}^2}{r} = (0.50)(9.8) + \frac{(0.50)(7.94)^2}{1.2} = 4.9 + \frac{31.5}{1.2} = 4.9 + 26.3 = 31.2\;\text{N}
\]

Notice: the tension at the bottom (31.2\,N) is much larger than at the top (1.77\,N). This is why strings and chains are most likely to break at the bottom of a vertical circle.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Comparison Table: Horizontal vs.\ Vertical Circular Motion}

\begin{center}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Horizontal Circle} & \textbf{Vertical Circle} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Speed & Constant (uniform) & Varies (fastest at bottom, slowest at top) \\
\midrule
Gravity's role & Perpendicular to motion (doesn't affect speed) & Along the radial direction (helps or hinders centripetal acceleration) \\
\midrule
Tension/Normal force & Constant & Varies---largest at bottom, smallest at top \\
\midrule
Energy conservation & Not needed (speed constant) & Essential for finding speed at different points \\
\midrule
Minimum speed condition & Any speed (as long as force is available) & $v_{\min} = \sqrt{gr}$ at the top \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Summary of Circular Motion}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Circular Motion:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Uniform circular motion has constant speed but changing velocity (direction changes).
\item Centripetal acceleration always points toward the center: $a_c = v^2/r$.
\item Centripetal force is provided by real forces (tension, friction, gravity, normal force)---never draw it as a separate force.
\item Speed and period are related by $v = 2\pi r / T$.
\item In vertical circles, use energy conservation to find speed at different points, then apply Newton's second law radially.
\item The minimum speed at the top of a vertical circle is $v_{\min} = \sqrt{gr}$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.1:} A car travels around a circular track of radius 100\,m at a constant speed of 20\,m/s. What is the centripetal acceleration?

\textbf{(A)} 0.2\,m/s$^2$ \quad \textbf{(B)} 2.0\,m/s$^2$ \quad \textbf{(C)} 4.0\,m/s$^2$ \quad \textbf{(D)} 400\,m/s$^2$

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} $a_c = v^2/r = (20)^2/100 = 400/100 = 4.0$\,m/s$^2$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.2:} An object moves in a circle at constant speed. Which statement is true?

\textbf{(A)} The object has zero acceleration \quad \textbf{(B)} The net force on the object is zero \quad \textbf{(C)} The velocity is constant \quad \textbf{(D)} The acceleration is directed toward the center

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (D)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Constant speed $\neq$ constant velocity. The direction changes continuously, so there is a centripetal acceleration directed toward the center. Since there is acceleration, there must be a net force (eliminating A and B). The velocity changes direction, so it is not constant (eliminating C).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.3:} A 0.30\,kg ball is swung in a horizontal circle on a 0.80\,m string at 5.0\,m/s. What is the tension in the string?

\textbf{(A)} 1.9\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 3.8\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 9.4\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 18
\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The tension provides the centripetal force:
\[
T = \frac{mv^2}{r} = \frac{(0.30)(5.0)^2}{0.80} = \frac{(0.30)(25)}{0.80} = \frac{7.5}{0.80} = 9.4\;\text{N}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.4:} If the radius of a circular path is doubled while the speed remains the same, what happens to the centripetal acceleration?

\textbf{(A)} It doubles \quad \textbf{(B)} It is halved \quad \textbf{(C)} It quadruples \quad \textbf{(D)} It stays the same

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} $a_c = v^2/r$. If $r \to 2r$ and $v$ stays the same: $a_c' = v^2/(2r) = \frac{1}{2}(v^2/r) = \frac{1}{2}a_c$. The centripetal acceleration is halved.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.5:} A roller coaster car of mass 500\,kg is at the top of a vertical loop of radius 10\,m. The speed at the top is 12\,m/s. What is the normal force from the track on the car at the top?

\textbf{(A)} 2300\,N \quad \textbf{(B)} 4900\,N \quad \textbf{(C)} 7200\,N \quad \textbf{(D)} 12100\,N

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (A)}

\textbf{Rationale:} At the top of the loop, both gravity and the normal force point toward the center (downward):
\[
N + mg = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies N = \frac{mv^2}{r} - mg = \frac{(500)(144)}{10} - (500)(9.8) = 7200 - 4900 = 2300\;\text{N}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.6:} What is the minimum speed a roller coaster car must have at the top of a vertical loop of radius 8.0\,m to maintain contact with the track?

\textbf{(A)} 4.4\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 6.3\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 8.9\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 12.5\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} At minimum speed, $N = 0$, so gravity alone provides centripetal force:
\[
mg = \frac{mv_{\min}^2}{r} \implies v_{\min} = \sqrt{gr} = \sqrt{(9.8)(8.0)} = \sqrt{78.4} = 8.9\;\text{m/s}
\]
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.7.7:} A satellite orbits Earth in a circular orbit. What provides the centripetal force?

\textbf{(A)} The satellite's engines \quad \textbf{(B)} Air resistance \quad \textbf{(C)} Gravity \quad \textbf{(D)} The satellite's momentum

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} In orbit, the only significant force acting on the satellite is Earth's gravitational pull, which is directed toward Earth's center---exactly the centripetal direction needed for circular motion.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Scenario: Vertical Loop Design}

An engineer is designing a vertical loop for a roller coaster. The loop has a radius of 15\,m.

\textbf{Part A:} What is the minimum speed the car must have at the top of the loop to maintain contact with the track?

\textbf{Part B:} Using energy conservation, find the minimum speed the car must have at the bottom of the loop to successfully complete the loop. (Assume the diameter of the loop is $2r = 30$\,m, so the top is 30\,m above the bottom.)

\textbf{Part C:} At the bottom of the loop, the car travels at the speed found in Part B. Find the normal force on a 70\,kg rider at the bottom. (At the bottom, the normal force points up and gravity points down, so $N - mg = mv^2/r$.)

\textbf{Part D:} Express the normal force at the bottom as a multiple of the rider's weight. Would this be comfortable?

\tcblower
\textbf{Part A:}
\[
v_{\min,\text{top}} = \sqrt{gr} = \sqrt{(9.8)(15)} = \sqrt{147} = 12.1\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Part B:} Using energy conservation from bottom to top:
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{bottom}}^2 = \frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{top}}^2 + mg(2r)
\]
\[
v_{\text{bottom}} = \sqrt{v_{\text{top}}^2 + 4gr} = \sqrt{(12.1)^2 + 4(9.8)(15)} = \sqrt{147 + 588} = \sqrt{735} = 27.1\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Part C:} At the bottom:
\[
N - mg = \frac{mv_{\text{bottom}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
N = mg + \frac{mv_{\text{bottom}}^2}{r} = (70)(9.8) + \frac{(70)(735)}{15} = 686 + 3430 = 4116\;\text{N}
\]

\textbf{Part D:}
\[
\frac{N}{mg} = \frac{4116}{686} = 6.0
\]

The rider experiences a normal force of $6g$---six times their weight! This would be extremely uncomfortable and potentially dangerous. Real roller coasters are designed to keep the g-force below about $4g$ to $5g$ for safety, which means the loop radius must be larger or the shape must be modified (real loops use a ``clothoid'' or teardrop shape, not a perfect circle).
\end{exercise}

%========================================================================
% SECTION 3.8: RELATIVE MOTION
%========================================================================

\section{Relative Motion}

\subsection{What Is Relative Motion?}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Relative motion} describes how the velocity of an object depends on the \emph{reference frame} of the observer. A \textbf{reference frame} is a coordinate system attached to a particular observer. The velocity of object A as measured by observer B is called the \textbf{velocity of A relative to B}, written $\vec{v}_{A/B}$.
\end{definition}

All motion is relative. When you say ``the car is going 60\,mph,'' you implicitly mean 60\,mph \emph{relative to the ground}. A passenger inside the car would say the car has zero velocity (relative to them). Both statements are correct---they just use different reference frames.

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you're walking forward on a moving bus. To someone sitting on the bus, you're walking at 1\,m/s. But to someone standing on the sidewalk watching the bus go by at 10\,m/s, you're moving at 11\,m/s (your walking speed plus the bus speed). Your velocity depends on \emph{who is watching}---that's relative motion. The person on the sidewalk and the person on the bus are in different reference frames, and they measure different velocities for you.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{The Relative Velocity Equation}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Relative Velocity Addition:} If you know the velocity of A relative to B, and the velocity of B relative to C, then the velocity of A relative to C is:
\[
\boxed{\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}}
\]
This is a \textbf{vector equation}---directions matter! The subscripts chain together: the inner subscripts (B) cancel, leaving A/C.
\end{theorem}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Subscript Chaining Rule:} Think of the subscripts like a chain:
\[
\vec{v}_{A/\cancel{B}} + \vec{v}_{\cancel{B}/C} = \vec{v}_{A/C}
\]
The ``B'' on the right of the first term cancels with the ``B'' on the left of the second term. This makes it easy to set up the equation correctly every time.

Also, reversing the subscripts reverses the velocity:
\[
\vec{v}_{B/A} = -\vec{v}_{A/B}
\]
If A sees B moving east at 5\,m/s, then B sees A moving west at 5\,m/s.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{One-Dimensional Relative Motion}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 1: Walking on a Train}

A passenger walks at 2\,m/s toward the front of a train that is moving at 30\,m/s relative to the ground. What is the passenger's velocity relative to the ground?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Let P = passenger, T = train, G = ground.

We know:
\begin{itemize}
\item $v_{P/T} = +2$\,m/s (passenger relative to train, forward)
\item $v_{T/G} = +30$\,m/s (train relative to ground, forward)
\end{itemize}

Using the relative velocity equation:
\[
v_{P/G} = v_{P/T} + v_{T/G} = 2 + 30 = 32\;\text{m/s (forward, relative to ground)}
\]
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 2: Walking Toward the Back}

The same passenger now walks at 2\,m/s toward the \emph{back} of the train. What is the passenger's velocity relative to the ground?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Now $v_{P/T} = -2$\,m/s (backward relative to train).

\[
v_{P/G} = v_{P/T} + v_{T/G} = (-2) + 30 = 28\;\text{m/s (forward, relative to ground)}
\]

The passenger is still moving forward relative to the ground, just more slowly than the train.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 3: Two Cars Approaching Each Other}

Car A travels east at 25\,m/s and Car B travels west at 30\,m/s, both relative to the ground. What is the velocity of Car A relative to Car B?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Let east be positive. We know:
\begin{itemize}
\item $v_{A/G} = +25$\,m/s
\item $v_{B/G} = -30$\,m/s
\end{itemize}

We need $v_{A/B}$. Using the chain rule:
\[
v_{A/B} = v_{A/G} + v_{G/B}
\]

We need $v_{G/B} = -v_{B/G} = -(-30) = +30$\,m/s.

\[
v_{A/B} = 25 + 30 = 55\;\text{m/s (east)}
\]

Car A approaches Car B at 55\,m/s---the speeds add because they're moving toward each other. This is why head-on collisions are so dangerous!
\end{solution}

\subsection{Two-Dimensional Relative Motion}

In two dimensions, the relative velocity equation becomes a vector addition problem.

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 4: Boat Crossing a River}

A boat can travel at 5.0\,m/s in still water. The river flows east at 3.0\,m/s. The boat aims straight north (perpendicular to the current). Find: (a) the boat's velocity relative to the ground, and (b) the angle the boat's actual path makes with north.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Let B = boat, W = water, G = ground. North is $+y$, east is $+x$.

\begin{itemize}
\item $\vec{v}_{B/W} = 5.0\,\hat{j}$\,m/s (boat relative to water, aimed north)
\item $\vec{v}_{W/G} = 3.0\,\hat{i}$\,m/s (water relative to ground, flowing east)
\end{itemize}

\[
\vec{v}_{B/G} = \vec{v}_{B/W} + \vec{v}_{W/G} = 3.0\,\hat{i} + 5.0\,\hat{j}\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(a) Speed relative to ground:}
\[
v_{B/G} = \sqrt{3.0^2 + 5.0^2} = \sqrt{9 + 25} = \sqrt{34} = 5.83\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(b) Angle east of north:}
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{3.0}{5.0}\right) = \tan^{-1}(0.60) = 31.0^\circ \text{ east of north}
\]

The current pushes the boat downstream. Even though the boat aims north, it actually travels northeast.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 5: Boat Aiming Upstream to Go Straight Across}

Same river (current = 3.0\,m/s east), same boat (speed in still water = 5.0\,m/s). At what angle upstream (west of north) should the boat aim to travel \emph{straight north} across the river? What is the boat's speed relative to the ground?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
We need $\vec{v}_{B/G}$ to point straight north ($+y$ direction only, no $x$-component).

Let the boat aim at angle $\theta$ west of north. Then:
\[
\vec{v}_{B/W} = -5.0\sin\theta\,\hat{i} + 5.0\cos\theta\,\hat{j}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{W/G} = 3.0\,\hat{i}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{B/G} = (-5.0\sin\theta + 3.0)\,\hat{i} + 5.0\cos\theta\,\hat{j}
\]

For straight-north travel, the $x$-component must be zero:
\[
-5.0\sin\theta + 3.0 = 0 \implies \sin\theta = \frac{3.0}{5.0} = 0.60 \implies \theta = 36.9^\circ
\]

The boat must aim $36.9^\circ$ west of north.

Speed relative to ground (only the $y$-component survives):
\[
v_{B/G} = 5.0\cos 36.9^\circ = 5.0(0.80) = 4.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

By aiming upstream, the boat sacrifices some of its speed to counteract the current. It crosses more slowly (4.0 vs.\ 5.0\,m/s) but travels in a straight line across the river.
\end{solution}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf
{Critical Distinction: Aiming vs.\ Traveling}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Aiming direction:} The direction the boat (or plane) points---this determines $\vec{v}_{B/W}$.
\item \textbf{Travel direction:} The actual path over the ground---this is $\vec{v}_{B/G}$.
\item These are \emph{different} whenever there is a current or wind!
\item If the current/wind speed exceeds the object's speed in the medium, the object \emph{cannot} travel straight across---it will always be swept downstream.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{Relative Motion in One Dimension}

Before tackling 2D problems, let's establish the principle in 1D.

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 6: Cars on a Highway}

Car A travels east at 80\,km/h and Car B travels east at 60\,km/h, both relative to the ground. What is the velocity of Car A relative to Car B?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
\[
\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_{A/G} - \vec{v}_{B/G} = 80 - 60 = 20\;\text{km/h (east)}
\]

From Car B's perspective, Car A is pulling ahead at 20\,km/h. This matches your everyday experience: if you're driving at 60 and someone passes you at 80, they seem to be going 20\,km/h relative to you.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 7: Head-On Approach}

Car A travels east at 80\,km/h and Car C travels \emph{west} at 70\,km/h. What is the velocity of Car A relative to Car C?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Taking east as positive: $\vec{v}_{A/G} = +80$\,km/h, $\vec{v}_{C/G} = -70$\,km/h.
\[
\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/G} - \vec{v}_{C/G} = 80 - (-70) = 150\;\text{km/h (east)}
\]

From Car C's frame, Car A approaches at 150\,km/h. This is why head-on collisions are so dangerous---the relative speed is the \emph{sum} of the individual speeds.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Relative Motion in Two Dimensions: Aircraft Problems}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Example 8: Airplane in a Crosswind}

A pilot wants to fly due north at a ground speed of 200\,km/h. The wind blows from the west at 50\,km/h (i.e., the wind velocity is 50\,km/h east). The plane's airspeed (speed relative to the air) is what value, and at what heading should the pilot aim?
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Let P = plane, A = air, G = ground. North = $+y$, east = $+x$.

We want: $\vec{v}_{P/G} = 200\,\hat{j}$\,km/h (due north).

Wind: $\vec{v}_{A/G} = 50\,\hat{i}$\,km/h (east).

The velocity addition equation:
\[
\vec{v}_{P/G} = \vec{v}_{P/A} + \vec{v}_{A/G}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{P/A} = \vec{v}_{P/G} - \vec{v}_{A/G} = 200\,\hat{j} - 50\,\hat{i} = -50\,\hat{i} + 200\,\hat{j}\;\text{km/h}
\]

The pilot must aim partly west to counteract the eastward wind.

Airspeed:
\[
v_{P/A} = \sqrt{50^2 + 200^2} = \sqrt{2500 + 40000} = \sqrt{42500} = 206.2\;\text{km/h}
\]

Heading angle west of north:
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{50}{200}\right) = \tan^{-1}(0.25) = 14.0^\circ \text{ west of north}
\]

The pilot aims $14.0^\circ$ west of north at an airspeed of 206.2\,km/h, and the wind carries the plane so it actually travels due north at 200\,km/h over the ground.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Relative Motion and Momentum}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Connecting Relative Motion to Conservation of Momentum:}

The principle of conservation of momentum is valid in \emph{any} inertial reference frame. However, the numerical values of momentum depend on which frame you choose. In collision problems, it is often strategic to work in the \textbf{center-of-mass frame} (where the total momentum is zero), solve the problem, and then transform back to the lab frame using relative velocity addition. This is an advanced but powerful technique.

For this course, the key connection is: when analyzing collisions between objects moving in different directions, you must carefully account for the velocity of each object \emph{in the same reference frame} before applying conservation of momentum.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Summary of Relative Motion}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Relative Motion:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Velocity is always measured relative to some reference frame.
\item The velocity addition equation $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ connects different frames.
\item The subscript chain rule: the inner subscripts must match (B appears as second in the first term and first in the second term).
\item To reverse a relative velocity: $\vec{v}_{B/A} = -\vec{v}_{A/B}$.
\item River/wind problems: decompose into components, set unwanted component to zero, and solve for the aiming angle.
\item Conservation of momentum works in any inertial frame---just be consistent about which frame you use.
\end{enumerate}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.8.1:} A boat can travel at 6.0\,m/s in still water. A river flows at 4.0\,m/s. If the boat aims straight across the river, what is its speed relative to the ground?

\textbf{(A)} 2.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} 5.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 7.2\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} 10.0\,m/s

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The boat's velocity relative to the ground has components: $v_x = 4.0$\,m/s (current) and $v_y = 6.0$\,m/s (across). Speed $= \sqrt{4.0^2 + 6.0^2} = \sqrt{16 + 36} = \sqrt{52} = 7.2$\,m/s.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.8.2:} Train A moves east at 30\,m/s and Train B moves west at 20\,m/s, both relative to the ground. What is the velocity of Train A relative to Train B?

\textbf{(A)} 10\,m/s east \quad \textbf{(B)} 10\,m/s west \quad \textbf{(C)} 50\,m/s east \quad \textbf{(D)} 50\,m/s west

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} Taking east as positive: $v_{A/B} = v_{A/G} - v_{B/G} = (+30) - (-20) = +50$\,m/s $= 50$\,m/s east. From Train B's perspective, Train A approaches at 50\,m/s.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.8.3:} A plane has an airspeed of 250\,km/h and aims due north. A wind blows from the north at 50\,km/h (i.e., the wind blows southward). What is the plane's ground speed?

\textbf{(A)} 200\,km/h \quad \textbf{(B)} 250\,km/h \quad \textbf{(C)} 255\,km/h \quad \textbf{(D)} 300\,km/h

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (A)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The wind opposes the plane's motion. $\vec{v}_{P/G} = \vec{v}_{P/A} + \vec{v}_{A/G}$. With north as positive: $v_{P/G} = 250 + (-50) = 200$\,km/h north.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.8.4:} A swimmer can swim at 2.0\,m/s in still water. The river current is 3.0\,m/s. Can the swimmer travel straight across the river?

\textbf{(A)} Yes, by aiming upstream at $42^\circ$ \quad \textbf{(B)} Yes, by aiming upstream at $34^\circ$ \quad \textbf{(C)} No, because the current is faster than the swimmer \quad \textbf{(D)} Yes, by swimming faster

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (C)}

\textbf{Rationale:} To go straight across, the swimmer needs $\sin\theta = v_{\text{current}}/v_{\text{swimmer}} = 3.0/2.0 = 1.5$. Since $\sin\theta$ cannot exceed 1, it is impossible. The swimmer will always be swept downstream because the current exceeds their swimming speed.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.8.5:} A boat aims $30^\circ$ west of north in a river that flows east at 4.0\,m/s. The boat's speed relative to the water is 8.0\,m/s. What is the eastward component of the boat's velocity relative to the ground?

\textbf{(A)} 0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(B)} $-4.0 + 4.0 = 0$\,m/s \quad \textbf{(C)} 0.0\,m/s \quad \textbf{(D)} All of the above

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (D)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The boat's velocity relative to water has eastward component $v_{B/W,x} = -8.0\sin 30^\circ = -4.0$\,m/s (westward). The current adds $+4.0$\,m/s eastward. Net eastward component: $-4.0 + 4.0 = 0$\,m/s. The boat travels straight north! All choices say the same thing.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Question 3.8.6:} Which of the following is the correct velocity addition equation?

\textbf{(A)} $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} - \vec{v}_{B/C}$ \quad \textbf{(B)} $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ \quad \textbf{(C)} $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{B/A} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ \quad \textbf{(D)} $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{C/B}$

\tcblower
\textbf{Correct Answer: (B)}

\textbf{Rationale:} The subscript chain rule requires the inner subscripts to match: $\vec{v}_{A/\mathbf{B}} + \vec{v}_{\mathbf{B}/C} = \vec{v}_{A/C}$. The ``B'' cancels like a fraction. Choice (A) has a minus sign error. Choice (C) reverses A/B to B/A. Choice (D) reverses B/C to C/B.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Unfolding Multi-Part Problem: Search and Rescue}

A rescue helicopter needs to reach a stranded boat that is 50\,km due east of the helicopter's base. A strong wind blows from the south at 60\,km/h (i.e., the wind blows northward). The helicopter's airspeed is 180\,km/h.

\textbf{Part A:} If the pilot aims due east, what will be the helicopter's actual velocity relative to the ground (magnitude and direction)?

\textbf{Part B:} At what angle south of east should the pilot aim to travel \emph{due east} to reach the boat directly?

\textbf{Part C:} When aiming as in Part B, what is the helicopter's ground speed?

\textbf{Part D:} How long does it take to reach the boat using the heading from Part B?

\textbf{Part E:} On the return trip (due west), the pilot again needs to counteract the northward wind. At what angle south of west should the pilot aim? What is the return ground speed and travel time?

\tcblower
\textbf{Part A:} If aiming due east: $\vec{v}_{H/A} = 180\,\hat{i}$, $\vec{v}_{A/G} = 60\,\hat{j}$ (northward wind).
\[
\vec{v}_{H/G} = 180\,\hat{i} + 60\,\hat{j}
\]
\[
v_{H/G} = \sqrt{180^2 + 60^2} = \sqrt{32400 + 3600} = \sqrt{36000} = 189.7\;\text{km/h}
\]
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{60}{180}\right) = 18.4^\circ \text{ north of east}
\]
The helicopter would drift north and miss the boat.

\textbf{Part B:} We need $\vec{v}_{H/G}$ to point due east (no northward component).

Let the pilot aim at angle $\theta$ south of east:
\[
\vec{v}_{H/A} = 180\cos\theta\,\hat{i} - 180\sin\theta\,\hat{j}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{H/G} = 180\cos\theta\,\hat{i} + (60 - 180\sin\theta)\,\hat{j}
\]

Set the $y$-component to zero:
\[
60 - 180\sin\theta = 0 \implies \sin\theta = \frac{60}{180} = \frac{1}{3} \implies \theta = 19.5^\circ \text{ south of east}
\]

\textbf{Part C:} Ground speed (only $x$-component):
\[
v_{H/G} = 180\cos 19.5^\circ = 180(0.943) = 169.7\;\text{km/h}
\]

\textbf{Part D:} Travel time:
\[
t = \frac{d}{v} = \frac{50}{169.7} = 0.295\;\text{h} = 17.7\;\text{min}
\
]

\textbf{Part E:} On the return trip, the helicopter must travel due west while the wind still blows north at 60\,km/h. The pilot must aim south of west.

Let the pilot aim at angle $\phi$ south of west:
\[
\vec{v}_{H/A} = -180\cos\phi\,\hat{i} - 180\sin\phi\,\hat{j}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{H/G} = -180\cos\phi\,\hat{i} + (60 - 180\sin\phi)\,\hat{j}
\]

Set the $y$-component to zero:
\[
60 - 180\sin\phi = 0 \implies \sin\phi = \frac{1}{3} \implies \phi = 19.5^\circ \text{ south of west}
\]

By symmetry, the angle is the same as the outbound trip! The ground speed is also the same:
\[
v_{H/G} = 180\cos 19.5^\circ = 169.7\;\text{km/h}
\]

Return travel time:
\[
t = \frac{50}{169.7} = 0.295\;\text{h} = 17.7\;\text{min}
\]

\textbf{Total mission time (travel only):} $17.7 + 17.7 = 35.4$\,min.

\textbf{Key insight:} When the crosswind is perpendicular to the desired travel direction, the correction angle and ground speed are the same in both directions. This is \emph{not} the case for headwinds vs.\ tailwinds!
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary of Relative Motion}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Relative Motion:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item All velocity is measured relative to a reference frame---there is no ``absolute'' velocity.
\item The velocity addition rule $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ is the master equation.
\item The subscript reversal rule $\vec{v}_{A/B} = -\vec{v}_{B/A}$ lets you flip perspectives.
\item In river/wind problems, identify three frames: object, medium, and ground.
\item ``Aiming direction'' $\neq$ ``travel direction'' whenever a current or wind exists.
\item To travel straight across a current, aim upstream at angle $\theta = \sin^{-1}(v_{\text{current}}/v_{\text{object in medium}})$.
\item This only works if $v_{\text{object}} > v_{\text{current}}$; otherwise the object cannot go straight across.
\end{enumerate}
\end{insightbox}

%========================================================================
%========================================================================
% PRACTICE PROBLEMS
%========================================================================
%========================================================================

\newpage

\section{Practice Problems}
\label{sec:practice}

\subsection*{Instructions}

For each group, the problem numbers correspond to the section topics covered in these notes. Show all work. Use the definitions of velocity and acceleration, conservation of energy, conservation of momentum, and forces. \textbf{Do not use the ``constant acceleration equations'' (kinematic equations) as standalone formulas.} Instead, derive any needed relationships from the definitions of velocity, acceleration, and energy conservation.

%---------- 9.1 Impulse ----------

\subsection{Section 9.1: Impulse}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.1.} A 0.40\,kg ball is at rest. A force of 50\,N is applied to it for 0.08\,s. Find the impulse delivered to the ball.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.2.} A force varies with time as follows: $F = 0$\,N for $t < 0$, $F = 120t$\,N for $0 \le t \le 0.05$\,s, and $F = 0$\,N for $t > 0.05$\,s. Calculate the impulse delivered during the nonzero force interval. (\emph{Hint:} The impulse is the area under the $F$-vs-$t$ graph, which is a triangle.)

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.3.} A baseball pitcher throws a 0.145\,kg ball. The force on the ball from the pitcher's hand varies during the throw, but the average force is 150\,N over a contact time of 0.06\,s. What impulse is delivered?

%---------- 9.2 Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem ----------

\subsection{Section 9.2: Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.1.} A 1200\,kg car travels at 25\,m/s. Calculate its momentum.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.2.} A 0.145\,kg baseball is thrown at 40\,m/s toward a batter, who hits it back at 50\,m/s in the opposite direction. If the contact time is 0.002\,s, find: (a) the change in momentum, and (b) the average force on the ball.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.3.} A 70\,kg person jumps from a wall and lands on the ground, coming to rest in 0.5\,s. If they were moving at 6\,m/s just before landing, what average force does the ground exert on them? Compare this to their weight.

%---------- 9.3 Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems ----------

\subsection{Section 9.3: Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.1.} A 2000\,kg truck traveling at 15\,m/s rear-ends a 1000\,kg car at rest. After the collision, the truck moves at 10\,m/s. What is the car's velocity after the collision? (\emph{Use the systematic problem-solving method: draw before/after diagrams, define the system, and apply the impulse-momentum theorem.})

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.2.} A 60\,kg astronaut floating at rest in space throws a 5.0\,kg tool kit at 8.0\,m/s. How fast does the astronaut recoil? What is the total momentum of the system before and after?

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.3.} Water from a fire hose hits a wall horizontally at 20\,m/s. The hose delivers 15\,kg of water per second. If the water does not bounce back (it simply runs down the wall), what force does the water exert on the wall?

%---------- 9.4 Conservation of Momentum ----------

\subsection{Section 9.4: Conservation of Momentum}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.1.} A 5.0\,kg cart moving at 4.0\,m/s to the right collides with a 3.0\,kg cart moving at 2.0\,m/s to the left. After the collision, the 5.0\,kg cart moves at 1.0\,m/s to the right. Find the velocity of the 3.0\,kg cart after the collision.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.2.} A 10\,g bullet is fired at 300\,m/s into a 2.0\,kg block of wood resting on a frictionless surface. The bullet embeds in the block. Find the velocity of the block-bullet system after the collision.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.3.} A cannon of mass 500\,kg fires a 5.0\,kg cannonball at 200\,m/s. What is the recoil velocity of the cannon?

%---------- 9.5 Inelastic Collisions ----------

\subsection{Section 9.5: Inelastic Collisions}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.1.} Two ice skaters stand face to face. Skater A (60\,kg) pushes Skater B (80\,kg). After the push, Skater A moves backward at 3.0\,m/s. Find Skater B's velocity. Is kinetic energy conserved? (\emph{Hint:} This is a ``reverse'' perfectly inelastic collision---an explosion.)

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.2.} A 1500\,kg car traveling at 20\,m/s collides head-on with a 1000\,kg car traveling at 15\,m/s in the opposite direction. They lock bumpers and move together. Find the velocity of the wreckage. What fraction of the initial kinetic energy is lost?

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.3.} A ballistic pendulum consists of a 10\,g bullet fired into a 2.0\,kg wooden block suspended from strings. After the bullet embeds, the block swings upward to a height of 0.10\,m. Find the bullet's speed. (\emph{Hint:} Use conservation of momentum for the collision, then conservation of energy for the swing.)

%---------- 9.6 Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions ----------

\subsection{Section 9.6: Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.1.} A 2.0\,kg ball moving east at 5.0\,m/s collides with a 3.0\,kg ball at rest. After the collision, the 2.0\,kg ball moves at 3.0\,m/s at $30^\circ$ north of east. Find the velocity (magnitude and direction) of the 3.0\,kg ball after the collision.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.2.} Two hockey pucks of equal mass undergo a glancing collision on frictionless ice. Puck A is initially moving at 8.0\,m/s; Puck B is at rest. After the collision, Puck A moves at $40^\circ$ from its original direction. If the collision is elastic, find the angle at which Puck B moves and the speeds of both pucks.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.3.} A 1200\,kg car traveling north at 20\,m/s collides with a 1500\,kg car traveling east at 15\,m/s at an intersection. They lock together. Find the velocity (magnitude and direction) of the wreckage.

%---------- 2.5 Motion with Constant Acceleration ----------

\subsection{Section 2.5: Motion with Constant Acceleration}

\textbf{Problem 2.5.1.} A car starts from rest and accelerates uniformly. After 8.0\,s its velocity is 24\,m/s. Using only the definition of acceleration, find the acceleration. Then using the definition of average velocity, find the distance traveled.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 2.5.2.} A ball is dropped from rest near Earth's surface. Using the definition of acceleration ($a = \Delta v / \Delta t$) and the definition of average velocity ($\bar{v} = \Delta x / \Delta t$), show that the distance fallen after time $t$ is $\Delta y = \frac{1}{2}g\,t^2$. (\emph{Do not cite a kinematic equation; derive the result from definitions.})

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 2.5.3.} A car traveling at 30\,m/s brakes uniformly to a stop in 6.0\,s. Using only definitions of acceleration and average velocity, find: (a) the acceleration, (b) the stopping distance.

%---------- 2.6 Solving One-Dimensional Motion Problems ----------

\subsection{Section 2.6: Solving One-Dimensional Motion Problems}

\textbf{Problem 2.6.1.} A ball is thrown straight up at 20\,m/s from a 45\,m tall building. Using the definition of acceleration and energy conservation (not kinematic equations), find: (a) the maximum height above the ground, (b) the time to reach maximum height, (c) the speed just before hitting the ground.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 2.6.2.} Two cars start from the same point. Car A starts from rest with acceleration 3.0\,m/s$^2$. Car B starts 2.0\,s later with acceleration 5.0\,m/s$^2$ (also from rest). Using definitions of velocity and acceleration, find when and where Car B catches Car A.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 2.6.3.} A stone is dropped from a bridge. A second stone is thrown downward from the same bridge 1.0\,s later at 15\,m/s. Using definitions and energy conservation, find when and where the two stones are at the same height.

%---------- 3.4 Motion in Two Dimensions ----------

\subsection{Section 3.4: Motion in Two Dimensions}

\textbf{Problem 3.4.1.} A particle has initial velocity $\vec{v}_0 = (3.0\,\hat{i} + 4.0\,\hat{j})$\,m/s and constant acceleration $\vec{a} = (1.0\,\hat{i} - 2.0\,\hat{j})$\,m/s$^2$. Using the definition of acceleration, find the velocity at $t = 3.0$\,s. Then find the speed and direction of motion.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.4.2.} An object moves in the $xy$-plane with acceleration $\vec{a} = -g\,\hat{j}$. At $t = 0$, $\vec{v}_0 = v_0\cos\theta\,\hat{i} + v_0\sin\theta\,\hat{j}$. Using only the definition of acceleration and average velocity, derive expressions for $x(t)$ and $y(t)$.

%---------- 3.5 Projectile Motion ----------

\subsection{Section 3.5: Projectile Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.5.1.} A ball is launched at 30\,m/s at $60^\circ$ above the horizontal from ground level. Using definitions of velocity and acceleration (not kinematic equations) and energy conservation, find: (a) the maximum height, (b) the total time of flight, (c) the range.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.5.2.} A stone is thrown horizontally at 15\,m/s from a 80\,m cliff. Using definitions and energy conservation, find: (a) the time to hit the ground, (b) the horizontal distance, (c) the speed just before impact.

%---------- 3.6 Projectile Motion: Solving Problems ----------

\subsection{Section 3.6: Projectile Motion --- Solving Problems}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.1.} A projectile is launched at 50\,m/s at $37^\circ$ above horizontal from the edge of a 100\,m cliff. Find: (a) the time to reach the base of the cliff, (b) the horizontal distance from the base, (c) the velocity (magnitude and direction) at impact. Use definitions and energy conservation.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.2.} A firefighter aims a hose at $45^\circ$ above horizontal. The water leaves the nozzle at 25\,m/s. A window is 20\,m away horizontally and 15\,m above the ground. Does the water reach the window? (\emph{Find the height of the water when it has traveled 20\,m horizontally.})

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.3.} An object is launched at speed $v_0$ at angle $\theta$ from the top of a building of height $H$. Using energy conservation, show that the speed at impact with the ground is $v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2g
H}$, independent of the launch angle $\theta$.

%---------- 3.7 Circular Motion ----------

\subsection{Section 3.7: Circular Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.1.} A car drives around a circular track of radius 200\,m at a constant speed of 25\,m/s. Find: (a) the centripetal acceleration, (b) the period of one revolution, (c) the net force on the car if its mass is 1200\,kg.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.2.} A 0.50\,kg ball is attached to a 1.2\,m string and swung in a vertical circle. At the top of the circle, the tension in the string is 3.0\,N. Using Newton's second law and energy conservation, find: (a) the speed at the top, (b) the speed at the bottom, (c) the tension at the bottom.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.3.} A satellite orbits Earth at a height where the radius of its orbit is $r = 6.77 \times 10^6$\,m. The gravitational acceleration at that height is $g' = 8.7$\,m/s$^2$. Find the orbital speed and the period of the orbit.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.4.} A roller coaster car goes over a circular hill of radius 15\,m. What is the maximum speed the car can have at the top of the hill without losing contact with the track?

%---------- 3.8 Relative Motion ----------

\subsection{Section 3.8: Relative Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.1.} A boat can travel at 8.0\,m/s in still water. A river flows east at 5.0\,m/s. The boat aims straight north. Find: (a) the velocity of the boat relative to the ground (magnitude and direction), (b) if the river is 200\,m wide (north--south), how far downstream does the boat land?

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.2.} Using the same boat and river from Problem 3.8.1, at what angle upstream (west of north) should the boat aim to travel straight across (due north)? What is the crossing time for the 200\,m wide river?

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.3.} An airplane has an airspeed of 250\,km/h. It needs to fly due south, but a wind blows from west to east at 80\,km/h. At what angle west of south should the pilot aim? What is the plane's ground speed?

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.4.} Car A travels north at 60\,km/h and Car B travels east at 80\,km/h. Find the velocity of Car A relative to Car B (magnitude and direction).

%---------- 9.1 Impulse ----------

\subsection{Section 9.1: Impulse}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.1.} A 0.145\,kg baseball is hit by a bat with a force that varies over time. The bat is in contact with the ball for 0.002\,s and the average force is 6500\,N. Find the impulse delivered to the ball.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.2.} A constant force of 120\,N acts on a 2.0\,kg object for 0.50\,s. Find: (a) the impulse, (b) the change in velocity.

%---------- 9.2 Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem ----------

\subsection{Section 9.2: Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.1.} A 0.060\,kg tennis ball traveling at 30\,m/s is hit by a racket and returns at 40\,m/s. Find: (a) the initial momentum, (b) the final momentum, (c) the impulse, (d) the average force if contact lasts 0.005\,s.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.2.} A 70\,kg person jumps from a wall and lands with a speed of 6.0\,m/s. Compare the average force on the person's legs if they: (a) land stiff-legged and stop in 0.01\,s, (b) bend their knees and stop in 0.30\,s.

%---------- 9.3 Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems ----------

\subsection{Section 9.3: Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.1.} A 1500\,kg car traveling at 20\,m/s crashes into a wall and comes to rest. The car crumples over a distance of 0.80\,m. Using the impulse-momentum theorem and energy considerations, find: (a) the impulse on the car, (b) the time of the collision (use the average velocity during the collision), (c) the average force on the car.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.2.} Water flows from a hose at a rate of 2.0\,kg/s and strikes a wall at 15\,m/s, coming to rest. What average force does the water exert on the wall?

%---------- 9.4 Conservation of Momentum ----------

\subsection{Section 9.4: Conservation of Momentum}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.1.} A 5.0\,kg cart moving at 4.0\,m/s collides with a stationary 3.0\,kg cart. After the collision, the 5.0\,kg cart moves at 1.5\,m/s. Find the velocity of the 3.0\,kg cart after the collision.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.2.} A 60\,kg astronaut floating in space throws a 5.0\,kg tool kit at 8.0\,m/s. What is the astronaut's recoil velocity?

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.3.} A 10\,g bullet is fired into a 2.0\,kg wooden block resting on a frictionless surface. The bullet embeds in the block, and the block (with bullet) slides at 1.5\,m/s. Find the bullet's initial speed.

%---------- 9.5 Inelastic Collisions ----------

\subsection{Section 9.5: Inelastic Collisions}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.1.} A 2000\,kg truck traveling at 15\,m/s collides head-on with a 1000\,kg car traveling at 20\,m/s in the opposite direction. They stick together after the collision. Find: (a) the velocity of the wreckage, (b) the kinetic energy lost in the collision.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.2.} A ballistic pendulum consists of a 10\,g bullet fired into a 5.0\,kg block suspended from strings. After the bullet embeds, the block rises 8.0\,cm. Find: (a) the speed of the block+bullet immediately after impact (use energy conservation), (b) the initial speed of the bullet (use conservation of momentum).

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.3.} Two objects ($m_1 = 3.0$\,kg at 6.0\,m/s east, $m_2 = 2.0$\,kg at 4.0\,m/s west) collide and stick together. Find the final velocity. Is this collision elastic or inelastic? Justify by comparing kinetic energies.

%---------- 9.6 Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions ----------

\subsection{Section 9.6: Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.1.} A 1500\,kg car traveling east at 20\,m/s collides with a 2000\,kg truck traveling north at 15\,m/s at an intersection. They lock together. Find: (a) the speed of the wreckage, (b) the direction of the wreckage.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.2.} A cue ball (0.17\,kg) traveling at 5.0\,m/s strikes a stationary billiard ball (0.16\,kg). After the collision, the cue ball moves at 3.0\,m/s at $30^\circ$ above its original direction. Find the speed and direction of the struck ball.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.3.} A 50\,kg skater moving north at 4.0\,m/s collides with a 70\,kg skater moving east at 3.0\,m/s. They hold onto each other after the collision. Find the speed and direction of the pair after the collision.

%---------- Mixed Review ----------

\subsection{Mixed Review}

\textbf{Problem MR.1.} A 2.0\,kg ball is launched from ground level at 25\,m/s at $53^\circ$ above the horizontal. At the highest point of its trajectory, it explodes into two equal pieces. One piece falls straight down (zero velocity immediately after explosion). Find: (a) the velocity of the other piece immediately after the explosion, (b) where the other piece lands relative to the launch point. Use conservation of momentum and energy methods (no kinematic equations).

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem MR.2.} A 5.0\,kg block slides down a frictionless ramp from a height of 3.0\,m and collides with a 3.0\,kg block at rest on a frictionless horizontal surface. The collision is perfectly inelastic. Find: (a) the speed of the 5.0\,kg block at the bottom of the ramp (use energy conservation), (b) the speed of the combined blocks after the collision, (c) the kinetic energy lost in the collision, (d) how far the combined blocks would travel up a second frictionless ramp (height).

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem MR.3.} A boat crosses a 500\,m wide river. The boat's speed in still water is 6.0\,m/s and the current flows at 4.0\,m/s. The boat aims upstream at an angle so that it crosses straight across. (a) Find the required angle. (b) Find the crossing time. (c) Upon reaching the other side, the boat picks up a 200\,kg crate and pushes off the dock. If the 500\,kg boat (with crate) starts from rest and the motor provides a net force of 1400\,N for 5.0\,s, find the impulse and the final speed.

\vspace{12pt}

\textbf{Problem MR.4.} A 0.25\,kg ball attached to a 0.80\,m string swings in a vertical circle. At the bottom, the speed is 6.0\,m/s. (a) Using energy conservation, find the speed at the top. (b) Find the tension at the top. (c) Find the tension at the bottom. (d) What is the minimum speed at the bottom for the ball to complete the full circle?

%========================================================================
% SECTION: SOLUTIONS TO PRACTICE PROBLEMS
%========================================================================

\newpage
\section{Solutions to Practice Problems}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.4: Motion in Two Dimensions}

\textbf{Problem 3.4.1.}

\begin{solution}
Using the definition of acceleration: $\vec{a} = \Delta\vec{v}/\Delta t$, so $\Delta\vec{v} = \vec{a}\,\Delta t$.

\[
\vec{v}(t) = \vec{v}_0 + \vec{a}\,t = (3.0\,\hat{i} + 4.0\,\hat{j}) + (1.0\,\hat{i} - 2.0\,\hat{j})(3.0)
\]
\[
= (3.0 + 3.0)\,\hat{i} + (4.0 - 6.0)\,\hat{j} = 6.0\,\hat{i} - 2.0\,\hat{j}\;\text{m/s}
\]

Speed: $v = \sqrt{6.0^2 + (-2.0)^2} = \sqrt{36 + 4} = \sqrt{40} = 6.32$\,m/s.

Direction: $\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{|-2.0|}{6.0}\right) = \tan^{-1}(0.333) = 18.4^\circ$ below the $+x$-axis.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.4.2.}

\begin{solution}
Starting from the definition of acceleration with $\vec{a} = -g\,\hat{j}$ (constant):

\textbf{Velocity:} $\vec{a} = \frac{\Delta\vec{v}}{\Delta t}$ gives $\Delta\vec{v} = \vec{a}\,\Delta t$, so:
\[
\vec{v}(t) = \vec{v}_0 + \vec{a}\,t = v_0\cos\theta\,\hat{i} + (v_0\sin\theta - gt)\,\hat{j}
\]

\textbf{Position:} The average velocity during interval $[0, t]$ with constant acceleration is:
\[
\vec{v}_{\text{avg}} = \frac{\vec{v}_0 + \vec{v}(t)}{2}
\]

Using $\Delta\vec{r} = \vec{v}_{\text{avg}}\cdot t$:

$x$-component: $v_{x}$ is constant at $v_0\cos\theta$, so $\bar{v}_x = v_0\cos\theta$ and:
\[
x(t) = (v_0\cos\theta)\,t
\]

$y$-component: $\bar{v}_y = \frac{v_0\sin\theta + (v_0\sin\theta - gt)}{2} = v_0\sin\theta - \frac{1}{2}gt$, so:
\[
y(t) = \left(v_0\sin\theta - \frac{1}{2}gt\right)t = v_0\sin\theta\,t - \frac{1}{2}gt^2
\]

These are the standard projectile equations, derived purely from the definitions of acceleration and average velocity---\emph{not} from memorized kinematic formulas.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.5: Projectile Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.5.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $v_0 = 30$\,m/s, $\theta = 60^\circ$, launched from ground level.

Components: $v_{0x} = 30\cos 60^\circ = 15.0$\,m/s, $v_{0y} = 
30\sin 60^\circ = 25.98$\,m/s.

\textbf{(a) Maximum height using energy conservation:}

At the maximum height, $v_y = 0$, so all vertical kinetic energy converts to potential energy:
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{0y}^2 = mgh_{\max}
\]
\[
h_{\max} = \frac{v_{0y}^2}{2g} = \frac{(25.98)^2}{2(9.8)} = \frac{675}{19.6} = 34.4\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{(b) Total time of flight:}

Using the definition of acceleration for the vertical component: $a_y = \Delta v_y / \Delta t$.

Time to reach the top (where $v_y = 0$):
\[
0 = v_{0y} - g\,t_{\text{up}} \implies t_{\text{up}} = \frac{v_{0y}}{g} = \frac{25.98}{9.8} = 2.65\;\text{s}
\]

By symmetry (returns to the same height), $t_{\text{total}} = 2t_{\text{up}} = 5.30$\,s.

\textbf{(c) Range:}

The horizontal velocity is constant ($v_x = v_{0x} = 15.0$\,m/s), so using the definition of velocity:
\[
R = v_{0x} \cdot t_{\text{total}} = 15.0 \times 5.30 = 79.5\;\text{m}
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.5.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $v_{0x} = 15$\,m/s, $v_{0y} = 0$ (horizontal launch), $h = 80$\,m.

\textbf{(a) Time to hit the ground:}

Vertical motion starts from rest ($v_{0y} = 0$) with acceleration $g$ downward. Using the definition of acceleration and average velocity:

$\bar{v}_y = \frac{0 + v_{fy}}{2} = \frac{v_{fy}}{2}$ and $v_{fy} = g\,t$, so $\bar{v}_y = \frac{gt}{2}$.

The vertical distance fallen equals $h$:
\[
h = \bar{v}_y \cdot t = \frac{gt}{2}\cdot t = \frac{1}{2}gt^2
\]
\[
t = \sqrt{\frac{2h}{g}} = \sqrt{\frac{2(80)}{9.8}} = \sqrt{16.33} = 4.04\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{(b) Horizontal distance:}

Using the definition of velocity (horizontal velocity is constant):
\[
x = v_{0x}\cdot t = 15.0 \times 4.04 = 60.6\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{(c) Speed just before impact using energy conservation:}
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_f^2 = \frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 + mgh
\]
\[
v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gh} = \sqrt{15^2 + 2(9.8)(80)} = \sqrt{225 + 1568} = \sqrt{1793} = 42.3\;\text{m/s}
\]

We can verify the direction: $v_{fx} = 15.0$\,m/s, $v_{fy} = gt = 9.8(4.04) = 39.6$\,m/s.
\[
v_f = \sqrt{15.0^2 + 39.6^2} = \sqrt{225 + 1568} = 42.3\;\text{m/s}\;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.6: Projectile Motion --- Solving Problems}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $v_0 = 50$\,m/s, $\theta = 37^\circ$, cliff height $H = 100$\,m.

Components: $v_{0x} = 50\cos 37^\circ = 39.9$\,m/s, $v_{0y} = 50\sin 37^\circ = 30.1$\,m/s.

Set the origin at the launch point, with up as positive. The ground is at $y = -100$\,m.

\textbf{(a) Time to reach the base of the cliff:}

Using the definition of acceleration: $v_y(t) = v_{0y} - gt = 30.1 - 9.8t$.

Using the definition of average velocity for position:
\[
y = v_{0y}\,t - \frac{1}{2}gt^2
\]

Set $y = -100$:
\[
-100 = 30.1\,t - 4.9\,t^2
\]
\[
4.9\,t^2 - 30.1\,t - 100 = 0
\]

Using the quadratic formula:
\[
t = \frac{30.1 \pm \sqrt{(30.1)^2 + 4(4.9)(100)}}{2(4.9)} = \frac{30.1 \pm \sqrt{906 + 1960}}{9.8} = \frac{30.1 \pm \sqrt{2866}}{9.8}
\]
\[
t = \frac{30.1 \pm 53.5}{9.8}
\]

Taking the positive root: $t = \frac{30.1 + 53.5}{9.8} = \frac{83.6}{9.8} = 8.53$\,s.

\textbf{(b) Horizontal distance:}
\[
x = v_{0x}\cdot t = 39.9 \times 8.53 = 340\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{(c) Velocity at impact using energy conservation:}
\[
v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gH} = \sqrt{50^2 + 2(9.8)(100)} = \sqrt{2500 + 1960} = \sqrt{4460} = 66.8\;\text{m/s}
\]

Direction: $v_{fx} = 39.9$\,m/s, $v_{fy} = v_{0y} - gt = 30.1 - 9.8(8.53) = 30.1 - 83.6 = -53.5$\,m/s (downward).
\[
\theta_f = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{53.5}{39.9}\right) = 53.3^\circ \text{ below the horizontal}
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $v_0 = 25$\,m/s, $\theta = 45^\circ$, target at $x = 20$\,m, $y = 15$\,m.

Components: $v_{0x} = 25\cos 45^\circ = 17.68$\,m/s, $v_{0y} = 25\sin 45^\circ = 17.68$\,m/s.

\textbf{Time to reach $x = 20$\,m:}
\[
t = \frac{x}{v_{0x}} = \frac{20}{17.68} = 1.131\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Height at that time:}

Using the definition of average velocity:
\[
y = v_{0y}\,t - \frac{1}{2}gt^2 = 17.68(1.131) - \frac{1}{2}(9.8)(1.131)^2
\]
\[
y = 19.99 - 6.27 = 13.7\;\text{m}
\]

The water reaches a height of 13.7\,m when it is 20\,m away horizontally. Since the window is at 15\,m, the water falls \textbf{short} by $15 - 13.7 = 1.3$\,m. \textbf{No}, the water does not reach the window.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.3.}

\begin{solution}
We use energy conservation. At the top of the building (height $H$ above ground), the object has:
\begin{itemize}
\item Kinetic energy: $\frac{1}{2}mv_0^2$ (this includes \emph{all} components of velocity, regardless of direction)
\item Potential energy: $mgH$
\end{itemize}

At the ground ($h = 0$):
\begin{itemize}
\item Kinetic energy: $\frac{1}{2}mv_f^2$
\item Potential energy: $0$
\end{itemize}

By conservation of energy:
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 + mgH = \frac{1}{2}mv_f^2
\]
\[
v_f^2 = v_0^2 + 2gH
\]
\[
\boxed{v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gH}}
\]

Notice that $v_0^2 = v_{0x}^2 + v_{0y}^2$ depends on the \emph{total} initial speed, not on how it is divided between horizontal and vertical components. The launch angle $\theta$ determines the split between $v_{0x}$ and $v_{0y}$, but $v_0^2 = (v_0\cos\theta)^2 + (v_0\sin\theta)^2 = v_0^2$ regardless of $\theta$.

Therefore, the final speed depends only on the initial speed $v_0$ and the height $H$, \textbf{not on the launch angle}. This is a powerful result of energy conservation!
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.7: Circular Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $r = 200$\,m, $v = 25$\,m/s, $m = 1200$\,kg.

\textbf{(a) Centripetal acceleration:}
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{25^2}{200} = \frac{625}{200} = 3.13\;\text{m/s}^2
\]

\textbf{(b) Period:}
\[
T = \frac{2\pi r}{v} = \frac{2\pi(200)}{25} = \frac{400\pi}{25} = 50.3\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{(c) Net force:}
\[
F_{\text{net}} = ma_c = 1200 \times 3.13 = 3750\;\text{N}
\]

This force is directed toward the center of the track and is provided by static friction between the tires and the road.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.50$\,kg, $L = 1.2$\,m, tension at top $T_{\text{top}} = 3.0$\,N.

\textbf{(a) Speed at the top:}

At the top of the circle, both gravity and tension point toward the center (downward):
\[
T_{\text{top}} + mg = \frac{mv_{\text{top}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
3.0 + (0.50)(9.8) = \frac{(0.50)v_{\text{top}}^2}{1.2}
\]
\[
3.0 + 4.9 = \frac{0.50\,v_{\text{top}}^2}{1.2}
\]
\[
7.9 = \frac{0.50\,v_{\text{top}}^2}{1.2} \implies v_{\text{top}}^2 = \frac{7.9 \times 1.2}{0.50} = 19.0
\]
\[
v_{\text{top}} = 4.36\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(b) Speed at the bottom using energy conservation:}

The bottom is a distance $2r = 2(1.2) = 2.4$\,m below the top. Using conservation of energy:
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{bot}}^2 = \frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{top}}^2 + mg(2r)
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot}}^2 = v_{\text{top}}^2 + 4gr = 19.0 + 4(9.8)(1.2) = 19.0 + 47.0 = 66.0
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot}} = 8.12\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(c) Tension at the bottom:}

At the bottom, tension points up (toward center) and gravity points down (away from center):
\[
T_{\text{bot}} - mg = \frac{mv_{\text{bot}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
T_{\text{bot}} = mg + \frac{mv_{\text{bot}}^2}{r} = (0.50)(9.8) + \frac{(0.50)(66.0)}{1.2}
\]
\[
T_{\text{bot}} = 4.9 + 27.5 = 32.4\;\text{N}
\]

Note how much larger the tension is at the bottom compared to the top---this is because at the bottom, the string must support the weight \emph{and} provide centripetal force, whereas at the top, gravity helps provide centripetal force.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.50$\,kg, $L = r = 1.2$\,m. We need the minimum speed at the bottom for the ball to complete the full vertical circle.

\textbf{Critical condition at the top:} The minimum speed at the top occurs when the tension is zero (the string goes slack at any lower speed):
\[
mg = \frac{mv_{\text{top,min}}^2}{r} \implies v_{\text{top,min}}^2 = gr = (9.8)(1.2) = 11.76\;\text{m}^2/\text{s}^2
\]

\textbf{Using energy conservation from bottom to top:}
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{bot,min}}^2 = \frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{top,min}}^2 + mg(2r)
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot,min}}^2 = v_{\text{top,min}}^2 + 4gr = gr + 4gr = 5gr
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot,min}} = \sqrt{5gr} = \sqrt{5(9.8)(1.2)} = \sqrt{58.8} = 7.67\;\text{m/s}
\]

This is a classic result: the minimum speed at the bottom of a vertical circle is $v_{\min} = \sqrt{5gr}$.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.8: Relative Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $v_{A/G} = 80$\,km/h east, $v_{B/G} = 100$\,km/h east.

Taking east as positive:

\textbf{Velocity of B relative to A:}
\[
v_{B/A} = v_{B/G} - v_{A/G} = 100 - 80 = +20\;\text{km/h (east)}
\]

From A's perspective, B is moving east at 20\,km/h (pulling ahead).

\textbf{Velocity of A relative to B:}
\[
v_{A/B} = v_{A/G} - v_{B/G} = 80 - 100 = -20\;\text{km/h (west)}
\]

From B's perspective, A is falling behind (moving west) at 20\,km/h. Note: $v_{A/B} = -v_{B/A}$, as expected.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Boat speed in still water $= 6.0$\,m/s, river current $= 4.0$\,m/s east, river width $= 120$\,m.

\textbf{Strategy 1: Aim straight across (north)}

$\vec{v}_{B/W} = 6.0\,\hat{j}$, $\vec{v}_{W/G} = 4.0\,\hat{i}$.
\[
\vec{v}_{B/G} = 4.0\,\hat{i} + 6.0\,\hat{j}
\]

Time to cross (determined by the northward component only):
\[
t = \frac{120}{6.0} = 20\;\text{s}
\]

Downstream drift: $\Delta x = 4.0 \times 20 = 80$\,m east.

Actual speed: $v_{B/G} = \sqrt{4.0^2 + 6.0^2} = \sqrt{52} = 7.21$\,m/s.

\textbf{Strategy 2: Aim upstream to go straight across}

Need the $x$-component of $\vec{v}_{B/G}$ to be zero.
\[
\sin\theta = \frac{v_{\text{current}}}{v_{\text{boat}}} = \frac{4.0}{6.0} = 0.667 \implies \theta = 41.8^\circ \text{ west of north}
\]

Northward component: $v_{B/G} = 6.0\cos 41.8^\circ = 6.0(0.745) = 4.47$\,m/s.

Time to cross: $t = \frac{120}{4.47} = 26.8$\,s.

No downstream drift, but it takes longer
.

\textbf{Comparison of strategies:}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
& \textbf{Aim North} & \textbf{Aim Upstream} \\
\midrule
Crossing time & 20\,s & 26.8\,s \\
Downstream drift & 80\,m & 0\,m \\
Ground speed & 7.21\,m/s & 4.47\,m/s \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Strategy 1 is faster but you land downstream. Strategy 2 takes longer but you arrive directly across.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Airplane airspeed $= 250$\,km/h, wind from the west at 50\,km/h (so wind velocity $= 50\,\hat{i}$\,km/h eastward), destination is due north.

We need $\vec{v}_{P/G}$ to point due north. Let the pilot aim at angle $\theta$ west of north.

\[
\vec{v}_{P/A} = -250\sin\theta\,\hat{i} + 250\cos\theta\,\hat{j}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{A/G} = 50\,\hat{i}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{P/G} = (-250\sin\theta + 50)\,\hat{i} + 250\cos\theta\,\hat{j}
\]

Set the $x$-component to zero:
\[
250\sin\theta = 50 \implies \sin\theta = 0.20 \implies \theta = 11.5^\circ \text{ west of north}
\]

Ground speed:
\[
v_{P/G} = 250\cos 11.5^\circ = 250(0.980) = 245\;\text{km/h}
\]

Travel time for 500\,km:
\[
t = \frac{500}{245} = 2.04\;\text{h} = 2\;\text{h}\;2.4\;\text{min}
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.8.4.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Train A moves east at 60\,km/h, Train B moves at $45^\circ$ north of east at 80\,km/h.

Express velocities in component form:
\[
\vec{v}_{A/G} = 60\,\hat{i}\;\text{km/h}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{B/G} = 80\cos 45^\circ\,\hat{i} + 80\sin 45^\circ\,\hat{j} = 56.57\,\hat{i} + 56.57\,\hat{j}\;\text{km/h}
\]

\textbf{Velocity of B relative to A:}
\[
\vec{v}_{B/A} = \vec{v}_{B/G} - \vec{v}_{A/G} = (56.57 - 60)\,\hat{i} + 56.57\,\hat{j} = -3.43\,\hat{i} + 56.57\,\hat{j}\;\text{km/h}
\]

Magnitude: $v_{B/A} = \sqrt{(-3.43)^2 + (56.57)^2} = \sqrt{11.76 + 3200.2} = \sqrt{3212} = 56.7$\,km/h.

Direction: $\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{3.43}{56.57}\right) = 3.5^\circ$ west of north.

From the perspective of a passenger on Train A, Train B appears to move almost due north at about 56.7\,km/h, drifting very slightly to the west.
\end{solution}

%========================================================================
% SOLUTIONS FOR MOMENTUM SECTIONS
%========================================================================

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.1: Impulse}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $F = 500$\,N, $\Delta t = 0.02$\,s.

Using the definition of impulse:
\[
J = F\,\Delta t = 500 \times 0.02 = 10\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

The impulse delivered to the ball is 10\,N$\cdot$s in the direction of the force.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.2.}

\begin{solution}
The impulse equals the area under the force--time curve.

For a triangular pulse from $t = 0$ to $t = 0.10$\,s with peak force $F_{\max} = 800$\,N at $t = 0.05$\,s:
\[
J = \text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2}(0.10)(800) = 40\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

The average force during this interval:
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{40}{0.10} = 400\;\text{N}
\]

Note: The average force equals half the peak force for a symmetric triangular pulse.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.2: Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.145$\,kg, $v_i = -40$\,m/s (toward batter), $v_f = +50$\,m/s (away from batter).

\textbf{Change in momentum:}
\[
\Delta p = m(v_f - v_i) = 0.145(50 - (-40)) = 0.145(90) = 13.05\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Impulse:} By the impulse--momentum theorem, $J = \Delta p = 13.05$\,N$\cdot$s.

\textbf{Average force:} Given contact time $\Delta t = 0.001$\,s:
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{13.05}{0.001} = 13{,}050\;\text{N} \approx 13\;\text{kN}
\]

This enormous force (about 2900\,lbs!) is why bat-ball collisions can break bats and why proper technique matters.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 70$\,kg, $v_i = 5.0$\,m/s (downward), $v_f = 0$ (stopped).

\textbf{Impulse needed:}
\[
J = m\Delta v = 70(0 - (-5.0)) = 350\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s (upward)}
\]

\textbf{Stiff landing} ($\Delta t = 0.01$\,s):
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{350}{0.01} = 35{,}000\;\text{N} = 35\;\text{kN}
\]
This is about 51 times body weight---enough to cause serious injury!

\textbf{Bent-knee landing} ($\Delta t = 0.30$\,s):
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{350}{0.30} = 1{,}167\;\text{N} \approx 1.2\;\text{kN}
\]
This is about 1.7 times body weight---safe and comfortable.

The impulse is the same in both cases (same $\Delta p$), but extending the time dramatically reduces the force. This is the physics behind airbags, crumple zones, and bending your knees when landing.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.3: Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 1500$\,kg, $v_i = 20$\,m/s, $v_f = 0$, $\Delta t = 0.15$\,s (collision with wall).

\textbf{Step 1: Define system and direction.} Let the initial direction of motion be positive.

\textbf{Step 2: Calculate initial and final momentum.}
\[
p_i = mv_i = 1500(20) = 30{,}000\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]
\[
p_f = mv_f = 1500(0) = 0
\]

\textbf{Step 3: Apply the impulse--momentum theorem.}
\[
J = \Delta p = p_f - p_i = 0 - 30{,}000 = -30{,}000\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Step 4: Find average force.}
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{-30{,}000}{0.15} = -200{,}000\;\text{N} = -200\;\text{kN}
\]

The negative sign indicates the force is opposite to the initial motion (a stopping force). The magnitude is 200\,kN---about 45,000\,lbs of force.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.060$\,kg (tennis ball), $v_i = 0$ (dropped, just before hitting ground use energy conservation to find speed).

Actually, let us restate: A 0.060\,kg tennis ball is dropped from 2.0\,m and bounces back to 1.5\,m. Find the impulse during the bounce.

\textbf{Speed just before hitting ground} (energy conservation):
\[
mgh_1 = \frac{1}{2}mv_1^2 \implies v_1 = \sqrt{2gh_1} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(2.0)} = 6.26\;\text{m/s (downward)}
\]

\textbf{Speed just after bouncing} (energy conservation):
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_2^2 = mgh_2 \implies v_2 = \sqrt{2gh_2} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(1.5)} = 5.42\;\text{m/s (upward)}
\]

Taking upward as positive: $v_i = -6.26$\,m/s, $v_f = +5.42$\,m/s.

\textbf{Impulse:}
\[
J = m\Delta v = 0.060(5.42 - (-6.26)) = 0.060(11.68) = 0.70\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s (upward)}
\]

Note how the impulse accounts for both stopping the ball and sending it back up. The ball doesn't return to the original height because some kinetic energy was lost (converted to heat and sound during the bounce).
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.4: Conservation of Momentum}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Astronaut mass $m_A = 80$\,kg, tool mass $m_T = 2.0$\,kg, thrown at $v_T = 10$\,m/s. Both initially at rest.

\textbf{System:} Astronaut + tool. No external forces in the direction of throwing (space, frictionless).

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
p_i = 0 \quad (\text{both at rest})
\]
\[
p_f = m_A v_A + m_T v_T = 0
\]
\[
80\,v_A + 2.0(10) = 0
\]
\[
v_A = \frac{-20}{80} = -0.25\;\text{m/s}
\]

The astronaut moves at 0.25\,m/s in the opposite direction to the thrown tool. This is how astronauts can maneuver in space---by throwing mass in one direction, they move in the other.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Cart A ($m_A = 3.0$\,kg, $v_{Ai} = 4.0$\,m/s right) collides with Cart B ($m_B = 2.0$\,kg, $v_{Bi} = -2.0$\,m/s, i.e., moving left). After collision, Cart A moves at $v_{Af} = 1.0$\,m/s right.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
m_A v_{Ai} + m_B v_{Bi} = m_A v_{Af} + m_B v_{Bf}
\]
\[
3.0(4.0) + 2.0(-2.0) = 3.0(1.0) + 2.0\,v_{Bf}
\]
\[
12 - 4 = 3 + 2\,v_{Bf}
\]
\[
8 = 3 + 2\,v_{Bf} \implies v_{Bf} = 2.5\;\text{m/s (right)}
\]

\textbf{Check: Is kinetic energy conserved?}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(3)(4)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(2)(2)^2 = 24 + 4 = 28\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(3)(1)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(2)(2.5)^2 = 1.5 + 6.25 = 7.75\;\text{J}
\]

$KE_f \neq KE_i$, so this is an \textbf{inelastic collision}. Energy lost: $28 - 7.75 = 20.25$\,J (converted to heat, sound, deformation).
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.5: Inelastic Collisions}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Car ($m_1 = 1200$\,kg, $v_1 = 15$\,m/s east) collides with truck ($m_2 = 3000$\,kg, at rest). They stick together (perfectly inelastic).

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
m_1 v_1 + m_2(0) = (m_1 + m_2)v_f
\]
\[
1200(15) = (1200 + 3000)v_f
\]
\[
18{,}000 = 4200\,v_f \implies v_f = 4.29\;\text{m/s east}
\]

\textbf{Kinetic energy lost:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(1200)(15)^2 = 135{,}000\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(4200)(4.29)^2 = 38{,}647\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = 135{,}000 - 38{,}647 = 96{,}353\;\text{J}
\]

\textbf{Fraction lost:} $96{,}353 / 135{,}000 = 71.4\%$ of the kinetic energy was lost to deformation, heat, and sound.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Ballistic pendulum. Bullet ($m = 0.010$\,kg, $v_0 = ?$) embeds in block ($M = 2.0$\,kg) on a string. The block+bullet rises to height $h = 0.15$\,m.

\textbf{Step 1: After collision, use energy conservation} to find the speed of the block+bullet just after collision:
\[
\frac{1}{2}(m + M)V^2 = (m + M)gh
\]
\[
V = \sqrt{2gh} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(0.15)} = \sqrt{2.94} = 1.71\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Step 2: Use conservation of momentum} for the collision:
\[
mv_0 = (m + M)V
\]
\[
0.010\,v_0 = (0.010 + 2.0
)(1.71)
\]
\[
v_0 = \frac{(2.010)(1.71)}{0.010} = \frac{3.437}{0.010} = 343.7\;\text{m/s}
\]

The bullet was traveling at approximately 344\,m/s before impact.

\textbf{Key insight:} We \emph{cannot} use energy conservation during the collision (it's perfectly inelastic---energy is lost). We \emph{can} use energy conservation for the pendulum swing (no energy lost to deformation during the swing). This two-step approach is the hallmark of ballistic pendulum problems.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Two ice skaters push off from rest. Skater A ($m_A = 50$\,kg) moves at 3.0\,m/s to the right. Skater B has mass $m_B = 70$\,kg.

This is an ``explosion'' (reverse perfectly inelastic collision). Initial momentum is zero.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
0 = m_A v_A + m_B v_B
\]
\[
0 = 50(3.0) + 70\,v_B \implies v_B = -\frac{150}{70} = -2.14\;\text{m/s (left)}
\]

\textbf{Energy released:}
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(50)(3.0)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(70)(2.14)^2 = 225 + 160.7 = 385.7\;\text{J}
\]

This energy came from the internal energy of the skaters (chemical energy in muscles converted to kinetic energy).
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.6: Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Ball A ($m_A = 0.3$\,kg, $v_{Ai} = 5.0$\,m/s in the $+x$ direction) collides with Ball B ($m_B = 0.3$\,kg, at rest). After collision, Ball A moves at $30^\circ$ above the $x$-axis at 4.0\,m/s.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum in $x$:}
\[
m_A v_{Ai} = m_A v_{Af}\cos 30^\circ + m_B v_{Bfx}
\]
\[
0.3(5.0) = 0.3(4.0)\cos 30^\circ + 0.3\,v_{Bfx}
\]
\[
1.5 = 1.039 + 0.3\,v_{Bfx} \implies v_{Bfx} = \frac{0.461}{0.3} = 1.537\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of momentum in $y$:}
\[
0 = m_A v_{Af}\sin 30^\circ + m_B v_{Bfy}
\]
\[
0 = 0.3(4.0)\sin 30^\circ + 0.3\,v_{Bfy}
\]
\[
0 = 0.6 + 0.3\,v_{Bfy} \implies v_{Bfy} = -2.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Speed and direction of Ball B:}
\[
v_{Bf} = \sqrt{1.537^2 + (-2.0)^2} = \sqrt{2.362 + 4.0} = \sqrt{6.362} = 2.52\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
\phi = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{2.0}{1.537}\right) = 52.4^\circ \text{ below the $+x$-axis}
\]

\textbf{Check if elastic:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.3)(5.0)^2 = 3.75\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(0.3)(4.0)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(0.3)(2.52)^2 = 2.4 + 0.953 = 3.353\;\text{J}
\]

$KE_f < KE_i$, so this collision is \textbf{inelastic} (about 10.6\% of kinetic energy was lost).
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: A 1500\,kg car traveling north at 20\,m/s collides with a 2000\,kg truck traveling east at 15\,m/s at an intersection. They stick together.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum in $x$ (east):}
\[
p_x = m_{\text{truck}}\,v_{\text{truck}} = 2000(15) = 30{,}000\;\text{kg\,m/s}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of momentum in $y$ (north):}
\[
p_y = m_{\text{car}}\,v_{\text{car}} = 1500(20) = 30{,}000\;\text{kg\,m/s}
\]

\textbf{After collision:}
\[
(m_{\text{car}} + m_{\text{truck}})v_{fx} = 30{,}000 \implies v_{fx} = \frac{30{,}000}{3500} = 8.57\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
(m_{\text{car}} + m_{\text{truck}})v_{fy} = 30{,}000 \implies v_{fy} = \frac{30{,}000}{3500} = 8.57\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Final speed:}
\[
v_f = \sqrt{8.57^2 + 8.57^2} = 8.57\sqrt{2} = 12.12\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Direction:}
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{8.57}{8.57}\right) = 45^\circ \text{ north of east}
\]

\textbf{Kinetic energy lost:}
\[
