KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(1500)(20)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(2000)(15)^2 = 300{,}000 + 225{,}000 = 525{,}000\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(3500)(12.12)^2 = \frac{1}{2}(3500)(146.9) = 257{,}143\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = 525{,}000 - 257{,}143 = 267{,}857\;\text{J}
\]

About 51\% of the kinetic energy was lost---a characteristic feature of perfectly inelastic collisions.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Firecracker (mass $M = 0.5$\,kg) at rest explodes into three pieces: piece 1 ($m_1 = 0.2$\,kg) moves at 10\,m/s in the $+x$ direction, piece 2 ($m_2 = 0.15$\,kg) moves at 12\,m/s in the $+y$ direction. Find the velocity of piece 3.

$m_3 = M - m_1 - m_2 = 0.5 - 0.2 - 0.15 = 0.15$\,kg.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum (initial momentum = 0):}

$x$-component:
\[
0 = m_1(10) + m_2(0) + m_3 v_{3x} \implies 0 = 2.0 + 0.15\,v_{3x} \implies v_{3x} = -13.33\;\text{m/s}
\]

$y$-component:
\[
0 = m_1(0) + m_2(12) + m_3 v_{3y} \implies 0 = 1.8 + 0.15\,v_{3y} \implies v_{3y} = -12.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Speed and direction of piece 3:}
\[
v_3 = \sqrt{(-13.33)^2 + (-12.0)^2} = \sqrt{177.7 + 144} = \sqrt{321.7} = 17.94\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
\theta = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{12.0}{13.33}\right) = 42.0^\circ
\]

Piece 3 moves at 17.94\,m/s in a direction $42.0^\circ$ below the $-x$-axis (into the third quadrant), which makes physical sense---it must balance the momentum of the other two pieces.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 2.5: Motion with Constant Acceleration}

\textbf{Problem 2.5.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Car accelerates from rest ($v_0 = 0$) to 25\,m/s in 8.0\,s with constant acceleration.

\textbf{(a) Acceleration} from the definition:
\[
a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t} = \frac{25 - 0}{8.0} = 3.125\;\text{m/s}^2
\]

\textbf{(b) Distance traveled} using average velocity:
\[
v_{\text{avg}} = \frac{v_0 + v}{2} = \frac{0 + 25}{2} = 12.5\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
d = v_{\text{avg}} \times t = 12.5 \times 8.0 = 100\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{Alternative using energy:} The work done equals the kinetic energy gained. If we know $F = ma$ and $W = Fd$:
\[
Fd = \frac{1}{2}mv^2 \implies mad = \frac{1}{2}mv^2 \implies d = \frac{v^2}{2a} = \frac{(25)^2}{2(3.125)} = \frac{625}{6.25} = 100\;\text{m} \;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 2.5.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Ball dropped from rest ($v_0 = 0$), $a = g = 9.8$\,m/s$^2$ downward, falls for $t = 3.0$\,s.

\textbf{(a) Velocity} from the definition of acceleration:
\[
v = v_0 + a\,t = 0 + 9.8(3.0) = 29.4\;\text{m/s downward}
\]

\textbf{(b) Distance fallen} using average velocity:
\[
v_{\text{avg}} = \frac{0 + 29.4}{2} = 14.7\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
d = v_{\text{avg}} \times t = 14.7 \times 3.0 = 44.1\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{(c) Verification using energy conservation:}

The potential energy lost equals kinetic energy gained:
\[
mgh = \frac{1}{2}mv^2 \implies h = \frac{v^2}{2g} = \frac{(29.4)^2}{2(9.8)} = \frac{864.36}{19.6} = 44.1\;\text{m} \;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 2.5.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Ball thrown upward at $v_0 = 20$\,m/s. Find maximum height and total time in air.

\textbf{Maximum height using energy conservation:}
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 = mgh_{\max} \implies h_{\max} = \frac{v_0^2}{2g} = \frac{(20)^2}{2(9.8)} = \frac{400}{19.6} = 20.4\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{Time to reach maximum height} from the definition of acceleration (at the top, $v = 0$):
\[
a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t} \implies \Delta t = \frac{\Delta v}{a} = \frac{0 - 20}{-9.8} = 2.04\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Total time in air:} By symmetry, $t_{\text{total}} = 2 \times 2.04 = 4.08$\,s.

\textbf{Verification:} At $t = 4.08$\,s, the velocity should be $-20$\,m/s (same speed, opposite direction):
\[
v = 20 + (-9.8)(4.08) = 20 - 39.98 \approx -20\;\text{m/s} \;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 2.6: Solving One-Dimensional Motion Problems}

\textbf{Problem 2.6.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Car brakes from 30\,m/s and decelerates at $a = -5.0$\,m/s$^2$. Find stopping distance and time.

\textbf{Stopping time} from the definition of acceleration:
\[
v = v_0 + at \implies 0 = 30 + (-5.0)t \implies t = 6.0\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Stopping distance} using average velocity:
\[
v_{\text{avg}} = \frac{30 + 0}{2} = 15\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
d = v_{\text{avg}} \times t = 15 \times 6.0 = 90\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{Verification with energy:} The work done by friction equals the kinetic energy lost:
\[
Fd = \frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 \implies |ma|d = \frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 \implies d = \frac{v_0^2}{2|a|} = \frac{900}{10} = 90\;\text{m} \;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 2.6.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Two cars start at the same point. Car A starts from rest with $a_A = 3.0$\,m/s$^2$. Car B passes at constant velocity $v_B = 20$\,m/s at $t = 0$. When does A catch B?

\textbf{Position of Car A} (using average velocity with $v_0 = 0$, $v = a_A t$):
\[
v_{\text{avg},A} = \frac{0 + a_A t}{2} = \frac{a_A t}{2}
\]
\[
x_A = v_{\text{avg},A} \cdot t = \frac{1}{2}a_A t^2
\]

\textbf{Position of Car B:}
\[
x_B = v_B\,t = 20t
\]

\textbf{Set equal:}
\[
\frac{1}{2}(3.0)t^2 = 20t \implies 1.5t^2 = 20t \implies 1.5t = 20 \implies t = 13.33\;\text{s}
\]

(We discard $t = 0$, the trivial solution.)

\textbf{Position where they meet:}
\[
x = 20(13.33) = 266.7\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{Speed of Car A at that moment:}
\[
v_A = a_A t = 3.0(13.33) = 40\;\text{m/s}
\]

Car A is traveling at \emph{twice} Car B's speed when it catches up. This makes sense: Car A's average speed over the interval must equal Car B's constant speed ($20$\,m/s), which means Car A's final speed must be $40$\,m/s (since average $= (0 + 40)/2 = 20$).
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 2.6.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Elevator starts from rest, accelerates upward at $2.0$\,m/s$^2$ for 5.0\,s, then moves at constant velocity for 10.0\,s, then decelerates at $-2.0$\,m/s$^2$ to rest.

\textbf{Phase 1: Acceleration} ($t_1 = 5.0$\,s, $a = 2.0$\,m/s$^2$, $v_0 = 0$)

Final velocity: $v_1 = 0 + 2.0(5.0) = 10.0$\,m/s

Distance: $d_1 = \frac{0 + 10}{2}(5.0) = 25.0$\,m

\textbf{Phase 2: Constant velocity} ($t_2 = 10.0$\,s, $v = 10.0$\,m/s)

Distance: $d_2 
= 10.0(10.0) = 100.0$\,m

\textbf{Phase 3: Deceleration} ($a = -2.0$\,m/s$^2$, $v_0 = 10.0$\,m/s, $v_f = 0$)

Time: $0 = 10.0 + (-2.0)t_3 \implies t_3 = 5.0$\,s

Distance: $d_3 = \frac{10.0 + 0}{2}(5.0) = 25.0$\,m

\textbf{Total distance:} $d = 25.0 + 100.0 + 25.0 = 150.0$\,m

\textbf{Total time:} $t = 5.0 + 10.0 + 5.0 = 20.0$\,s

\textbf{Average speed:} $\bar{v} = 150.0/20.0 = 7.5$\,m/s

\textbf{Energy check for Phase 1:} Work done by net upward force:
\[
W_{\text{net}} = F_{\text{net}} \cdot d_1 = ma \cdot d_1 = m(2.0)(25.0) = 50m
\]
\[
\Delta KE = \frac{1}{2}m(10)^2 - 0 = 50m \;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.4: Motion in Two Dimensions}

(Solutions for 3.4 were provided in the previous solutions section above.)

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.5: Projectile Motion}

(Solutions for 3.5 were provided in the previous solutions section above.)

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.6: Projectile Motion --- Solving Problems}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $v_0 = 50$\,m/s, $\theta = 37^\circ$ above horizontal, launched from a cliff of height $H = 100$\,m.

Components: $v_{0x} = 50\cos 37^\circ = 39.93$\,m/s, $v_{0y} = 50\sin 37^\circ = 30.09$\,m/s.

\textbf{(a) Time to reach the base of the cliff:}

Taking the launch point as origin with upward positive, the projectile hits ground when $y = -100$\,m.

Using the definition of acceleration and average velocity for the vertical direction:
\[
y = v_{0y}\,t - \frac{1}{2}g\,t^2
\]
\[
-100 = 30.09\,t - 4.9\,t^2
\]
\[
4.9\,t^2 - 30.09\,t - 100 = 0
\]

Using the quadratic formula:
\[
t = \frac{30.09 \pm \sqrt{(30.09)^2 + 4(4.9)(100)}}{2(4.9)} = \frac{30.09 \pm \sqrt{905.4 + 1960}}{9.8}
\]
\[
t = \frac{30.09 \pm \sqrt{2865.4}}{9.8} = \frac{30.09 \pm 53.53}{9.8}
\]

Taking the positive root: $t = \frac{30.09 + 53.53}{9.8} = \frac{83.62}{9.8} = 8.53$\,s.

\textbf{(b) Horizontal distance:}
\[
x = v_{0x}\,t = 39.93(8.53) = 340.6\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{(c) Velocity at impact using energy conservation:}
\[
v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gH} = \sqrt{50^2 + 2(9.8)(100)} = \sqrt{2500 + 1960} = \sqrt{4460} = 66.8\;\text{m/s}
\]

Direction: $v_{fx} = 39.93$\,m/s, $v_{fy} = v_{0y} - gt = 30.09 - 9.8(8.53) = 30.09 - 83.59 = -53.50$\,m/s.
\[
\theta_f = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{53.50}{39.93}\right) = 53.3^\circ \text{ below the horizontal}
\]

\textbf{Speed check:} $\sqrt{39.93^2 + 53.50^2} = \sqrt{1594 + 2862} = \sqrt{4456} = 66.8$\,m/s $\checkmark$
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Hose at $45^\circ$, $v_0 = 25$\,m/s. Window at $x = 20$\,m, $y = 15$\,m.

Components: $v_{0x} = 25\cos 45^\circ = 17.68$\,m/s, $v_{0y} = 25\sin 45^\circ = 17.68$\,m/s.

\textbf{Time to reach $x = 20$\,m:}
\[
t = \frac{x}{v_{0x}} = \frac{20}{17.68} = 1.131\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Height at that time:}
\[
y = v_{0y}\,t - \frac{1}{2}g\,t^2 = 17.68(1.131) - \frac{1}{2}(9.8)(1.131)^2
\]
\[
y = 19.99 - 6.26 = 13.73\;\text{m}
\]

The water reaches a height of 13.73\,m when it is 20\,m away horizontally. Since the window is at 15\,m, the water falls \textbf{short by 1.27\,m}. The water does \emph{not} reach the window.

\textbf{Alternative check using energy conservation:} At $x = 20$\,m, the speed is:
\[
v = \sqrt{v_0^2 - 2gy} = \sqrt{625 - 2(9.8)(13.73)} = \sqrt{625 - 269.1} = \sqrt{355.9} = 18.87\;\text{m/s}
\]
This is consistent with the water still moving (not yet at maximum height), confirming our trajectory calculation.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.6.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Object launched at speed $v_0$ at angle $\theta$ from height $H$ above ground. Show $v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gH}$.

\textbf{Using energy conservation:}

Take the ground as the reference level for potential energy.

\[
KE_i + PE_i = KE_f + PE_f
\]
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 + mgH = \frac{1}{2}mv_f^2 + 0
\]
\[
v_f^2 = v_0^2 + 2gH
\]
\[
\boxed{v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gH}}
\]

Notice that $\theta$ does not appear anywhere in this expression! The final \emph{speed} depends only on the initial speed and the height fallen, not on the direction of launch. This is a powerful result of energy conservation: energy is a scalar and does not depend on direction.

\textbf{Physical interpretation:} Whether you throw a ball straight up, horizontally, or at any angle from the same height with the same initial speed, it will hit the ground with the same final speed. Only the \emph{direction} of the velocity at impact will differ.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.7: Circular Motion}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Circular track, $r = 200$\,m, $v = 25$\,m/s, $m = 1200$\,kg.

\textbf{(a) Centripetal acceleration:}
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{(25)^2}{200} = \frac{625}{200} = 3.125\;\text{m/s}^2
\]

\textbf{(b) Period:}
\[
T = \frac{2\pi r}{v} = \frac{2\pi(200)}{25} = \frac{400\pi}{25} = 50.27\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{(c) Net force:}
\[
F_{\text{net}} = ma_c = 1200(3.125) = 3750\;\text{N directed toward the center}
\]

This net force is provided by static friction between the tires and the road.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.50$\,kg, $r = 1.2$\,m, tension at top $T_{\text{top}} = 3.0$\,N.

\textbf{(a) Speed at the top:}

At the top, both tension and gravity point toward the center (downward):
\[
T_{\text{top}} + mg = \frac{mv_{\text{top}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
3.0 + 0.50(9.8) = \frac{0.50\,v_{\text{top}}^2}{1.2}
\]
\[
3.0 + 4.9 = \frac{0.50\,v_{\text{top}}^2}{1.2}
\]
\[
7.9 = \frac{0.50\,v_{\text{top}}^2}{1.2} \implies v_{\text{top}}^2 = \frac{7.9 \times 1.2}{0.50} = 18.96
\]
\[
v_{\text{top}} = 4.35\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(b) Speed at the bottom using energy conservation:}

The height difference between bottom and top is $2r = 2.4$\,m.
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{bot}}^2 = \frac{1}{2}mv_{\text{top}}^2 + mg(2r)
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot}}^2 = v_{\text{top}}^2 + 4gr = 18.96 + 4(9.8)(1.2) = 18.96 + 47.04 = 66.0
\]
\[
v_{\text{bot}} = 8.12\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(c) Tension at the bottom:}

At the bottom, tension points up (toward center) and gravity points down (away from center):
\[
T_{\text{bot}} - mg = \frac{mv_{\text{bot}}^2}{r}
\]
\[
T_{\text{bot}} = mg + \frac{mv_{\text{bot}}^2}{r} = 0.50(9.8) + \frac{0.50(66.0)}{1.2}
\]
\[
T_{\text{bot}} = 4.9 + 27.5 = 32.4\;\text{N}
\]

The tension at the bottom is much larger than at the top because it must support the weight \emph{and} provide the centripetal force.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 3.7.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Satellite orbits Earth at height $h$ above the surface. $R_E = 6.37 \times 10^6$\,m, $g_{\text{surface}} = 9.8$\,m/s$^2$.

The orbital radius is $r = R_E + h$.

For a satellite in circular orbit, gravity provides the centripetal force:
\[
\frac{GMm}{r^2} = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies v^2 = \frac{GM}{r}
\]

Since $g_{\text{surface}} = GM/R_E^2$, we have $GM = g R_E^2$, so:
\[
v = \sqrt{\frac{g R_E^2}{R_E + h}}
\]

For $h = 300$\,km $= 3.0 \times 10^5$\,m:
\[
v = \sqrt{\frac{9.8(6.37 \times 10^6)^2}{6.67 \times 10^6}} = \sqrt{\frac{9.8 \times 4.058 \times 10^{13}}{6.67 \times 10^6}}
\]
\[
v = \sqrt{5.963 \times 10^7} = 7723\;\text{m/s} \approx 7.7\;\text{km/s}
\]

Period:
\[
T = \frac{2\pi r}{v} = \frac{2\pi(6.67 \times 10^6)}{7723} = 5425\;\text{s} \approx 90.4\;\text{min}
\]

This is consistent with the orbital period of the International Space Station.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 3.8: Relative Motion}

(Solutions for 3.8.1 and 3.8.2 were provided above. Continuing with 3.8.3.)

\textbf{Problem 3.8.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Airplane airspeed $= 250$\,km/h, wind from the west at 50\,km/h (so $\vec{v}_{\text{wind}} = 50\,\hat{i}$\,km/h east), destination due north.

The pilot must aim west of north to compensate. Let $\theta$ be the angle west of north.

\[
\vec{v}_{P/A} = -250\sin\theta\,\hat{i} + 250\cos\theta\,\hat{j}
\]
\[
\vec{v}_{P/G} = \vec{v}_{P/A} + \vec{v}_{A/G} = (-250\sin\theta + 50)\,\hat{i} + 250\cos\theta\,\hat{j}
\]

For the plane to travel due north, the $x$-component must be zero:
\[
-250\sin\theta + 50 = 0 \implies \sin\theta = \frac{50}{250} = 0.20 \implies \theta = 11.54^\circ
\]

The pilot must aim $11.5^\circ$ west of north.

Ground speed (northward component):
\[
v_{P/G} = 250\cos 11.54^\circ = 250(0.9798) = 244.9\;\text{km/h}
\]

If the destination is 500\,km due north:
\[
t = \frac{500}{244.9} = 2.04\;\text{hours} = 2\;\text{h}\;2.5\;\text{min}
\]
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.1: Impulse}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $F = 500$\,N, $\Delta t = 0.02$\,s.
\[
J = F\,\Delta t = 500(0.02) = 10\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
The impulse delivered to the ball is 10\,N$\cdot$s in the direction of the force.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.145$\,kg, $v_i = -40$\,m/s (toward pitcher), $v_f = +50$\,m/s (away from pitcher).

\textbf{Impulse from the impulse-momentum theorem:}
\[
J = \Delta p = m(v_f - v_i) = 0.145(50 - (-40)) = 0.145(90) = 13.05\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Average force} if contact time is $\Delta t = 0.001$\,s:
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{13.05}{0.001} = 13{,}050\;\text{N}
\]

This enormous force (about 1330\,kg equivalent weight!) explains why bats can break during a hit.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.1.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 60$\,kg, $v_i = 0$, $v_f = 5.0$\,m/s, $\Delta t = 0.50$\,s.

\[
J = m\Delta v = 60(5.0 - 0) = 300\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{J}{\Delta t} = \frac{300}{0.50} = 600\;\text{N}
\]

The ground ex
erts a force of 600\,N on the sprinter during the start. By Newton's third law, the sprinter pushes backward on the ground with 600\,N.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.2: Momentum and Impulse-Momentum Theorem}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.40$\,kg, $v = 20$\,m/s.
\[
p = mv = 0.40(20) = 8.0\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 1500$\,kg, $v_i = 25$\,m/s, $v_f = 0$, $\Delta t = 0.15$\,s (hitting a wall).

\[
\Delta p = m(v_f - v_i) = 1500(0 - 25) = -37{,}500\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{\Delta p}{\Delta t} = \frac{-37{,}500}{0.15} = -250{,}000\;\text{N}
\]

The magnitude is 250\,kN, directed opposite to the car's initial motion. This is approximately 17 times the car's weight---illustrating why car crashes are so destructive.

\textbf{With a crumple zone} ($\Delta t = 0.80$\,s):
\[
F_{\text{avg}} = \frac{-37{,}500}{0.80} = -46{,}875\;\text{N}
\]

The crumple zone reduces the force by a factor of $250{,}000/46{,}875 \approx 5.3$. Same impulse, but spread over a longer time.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.2.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 0.060$\,kg, dropped from $h = 1.5$\,m, bounces back to $h' = 1.0$\,m.

\textbf{Speed just before hitting floor} (energy conservation):
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_1^2 = mgh \implies v_1 = \sqrt{2gh} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(1.5)} = 5.42\;\text{m/s (downward)}
\]

\textbf{Speed just after bounce} (energy conservation):
\[
v_2 = \sqrt{2gh'} = \sqrt{2(9.8)(1.0)} = 4.43\;\text{m/s (upward)}
\]

Taking upward as positive: $v_i = -5.42$\,m/s, $v_f = +4.43$\,m/s.

\[
\Delta p = m(v_f - v_i) = 0.060(4.43 - (-5.42)) = 0.060(9.85) = 0.591\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s (upward)}
\]

\textbf{Kinetic energy lost:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.060)(5.42)^2 = 0.882\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(0.060)(4.43)^2 = 0.589\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = 0.882 - 0.589 = 0.293\;\text{J lost}
\]

Alternatively: the ball started at $h = 1.5$\,m and returned to $h' = 1.0$\,m, so energy lost $= mg(h - h') = 0.060(9.8)(0.5) = 0.294$\,J $\checkmark$.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.3: Solving Impulse and Momentum Problems}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Water hose delivers 15\,kg of water per second at $v = 20$\,m/s, water does not bounce back (sticks to wall).

\textbf{Force on wall:} Each second, the momentum delivered is:
\[
\Delta p = \frac{\Delta m}{\Delta t}\cdot v \cdot \Delta t = \dot{m}\,v\,\Delta t
\]

The average force equals the rate of momentum transfer:
\[
F = \frac{\Delta p}{\Delta t} = \dot{m}\,v = 15(20) = 300\;\text{N}
\]

If the water bounced straight back at the same speed, the force would double to 600\,N (since $\Delta v = 2v$).
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: $m = 2000$\,kg truck, $v_i = 0$, engine provides $F = 5000$\,N for $t = 10$\,s. Friction force $= 1000$\,N.

\textbf{Net force:} $F_{\text{net}} = 5000 - 1000 = 4000$\,N.

\textbf{Impulse-momentum theorem:}
\[
F_{\text{net}}\,\Delta t = m\,\Delta v
\]
\[
4000(10) = 2000(v_f - 0)
\]
\[
v_f = \frac{40{,}000}{2000} = 20\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Distance traveled} (using average velocity):
\[
v_{\text{avg}} = \frac{0 + 20}{2} = 10\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
d = v_{\text{avg}} \cdot t = 10(10) = 100\;\text{m}
\]

\textbf{Verification with energy:}
\[
W_{\text{net}} = F_{\text{net}} \cdot d = 4000(100) = 400{,}000\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = \frac{1}{2}(2000)(20)^2 = 400{,}000\;\text{J}\;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.3.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Astronaut ($m_A = 80$\,kg) throws toolbox ($m_T = 10$\,kg) at $v_T = 6.0$\,m/s relative to the space station. Both initially at rest.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum} (system initially at rest, so $p_i = 0$):
\[
0 = m_A v_A + m_T v_T
\]
\[
v_A = -\frac{m_T v_T}{m_A} = -\frac{10(6.0)}{80} = -0.75\;\text{m/s}
\]

The astronaut drifts at 0.75\,m/s in the opposite direction.

\textbf{Time to reach the ship 12\,m away:}
\[
t = \frac{d}{|v_A|} = \frac{12}{0.75} = 16\;\text{s}
\]

\textbf{Energy analysis:} All kinetic energy came from the astronaut's muscles:
\[
KE_{\text{total}} = \frac{1}{2}(80)(0.75)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(10)(6.0)^2 = 22.5 + 180 = 202.5\;\text{J}
\]

Most of the energy goes to the lighter toolbox, as expected.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.4: Conservation of Momentum}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Cannon ($M = 500$\,kg) fires cannonball ($m = 5.0$\,kg) at $v_b = 400$\,m/s.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum} (initially at rest):
\[
0 = Mv_C + mv_b \implies v_C = -\frac{mv_b}{M} = -\frac{5.0(400)}{500} = -4.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

The cannon recoils at 4.0\,m/s in the opposite direction.

\textbf{Kinetic energies:}
\[
KE_{\text{ball}} = \frac{1}{2}(5.0)(400)^2 = 400{,}000\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_{\text{cannon}} = \frac{1}{2}(500)(4.0)^2 = 4{,}000\;\text{J}
\]

The cannonball gets $400{,}000/404{,}000 = 99\%$ of the energy. The lighter object always gets the larger share of kinetic energy in an explosion.

\textbf{Why rockets work in space:} The rocket is the ``cannon'' and the exhaust gas is the ``cannonball.'' By expelling mass at high speed in one direction, the rocket gains momentum in the opposite direction. No air or surface is needed to ``push against''---conservation of momentum is the fundamental principle.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Cart A ($m_A = 3.0$\,kg, $v_{Ai} = 4.0$\,m/s right), Cart B ($m_B = 2.0$\,kg, $v_{Bi} = -2.0$\,m/s left). After collision, $v_{Af} = 1.0$\,m/s right.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
m_A v_{Ai} + m_B v_{Bi} = m_A v_{Af} + m_B v_{Bf}
\]
\[
3.0(4.0) + 2.0(-2.0) = 3.0(1.0) + 2.0\,v_{Bf}
\]
\[
12 - 4 = 3 + 2\,v_{Bf} \implies 8 = 3 + 2\,v_{Bf} \implies v_{Bf} = 2.5\;\text{m/s (right)}
\]

\textbf{Kinetic energy check:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(3)(4)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(2)(2)^2 = 24 + 4 = 28\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(3)(1)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(2)(2.5)^2 = 1.5 + 6.25 = 7.75\;\text{J}
\]

This is an inelastic collision: $28 - 7.75 = 20.25$\,J was lost to heat, sound, and deformation.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.4.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Two objects, $m_1 = 4.0$\,kg moving at $v_1 = 6.0$\,m/s and $m_2 = 8.0$\,kg at rest. After a perfectly elastic collision, find both final velocities.

For a 1D elastic collision, we use \textbf{both} conservation of momentum and conservation of kinetic energy.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
m_1 v_1 = m_1 v_{1f} + m_2 v_{2f}
\]
\[
4.0(6.0) = 4.0\,v_{1f} + 8.0\,v_{2f} \implies 24 = 4v_{1f} + 8v_{2f} \quad \text{...(1)}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of kinetic energy:}
\[
\frac{1}{2}m_1 v_1^2 = \frac{1}{2}m_1 v_{1f}^2 + \frac{1}{2}m_2 v_{2f}^2
\]
\[
4(36) = 4v_{1f}^2 + 8v_{2f}^2 \implies 144 = 4v_{1f}^2 + 8v_{2f}^2 \quad \text{...(2)}
\]

\textbf{Using the relative velocity trick} (valid for elastic collisions):
\[
v_1 - v_2 = -(v_{1f} - v_{2f}) \implies 6 - 0 = v_{2f} - v_{1f} \implies v_{2f} = v_{1f} + 6 \quad \text{...(3)}
\]

Substituting (3) into (1):
\[
24 = 4v_{1f} + 8(v_{1f} + 6) = 12v_{1f} + 48
\]
\[
12v_{1f} = -24 \implies v_{1f} = -2.0\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
v_{2f} = -2.0 + 6.0 = 4.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

Object 1 bounces back at 2.0\,m/s; object 2 moves forward at 4.0\,m/s.

\textbf{Verification:}
\[
p_f = 4(-2) + 8(4) = -8 + 32 = 24 = p_i\;\checkmark
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(4)(4) + \frac{1}{2}(8)(16) = 8 + 64 = 72 = KE_i\;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.5: Inelastic Collisions}

(Solutions for 9.5.1 and 9.5.2 were provided above. Continuing with 9.5.3.)

\textbf{Problem 9.5.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Two ice skaters push off from rest. Skater A ($m_A = 50$\,kg) moves right at 3.0\,m/s. Skater B ($m_B = 70$\,kg).

\textbf{Conservation of momentum} (initially at rest, $p_i = 0$):
\[
0 = m_A v_A + m_B v_B
\]
\[
0 = 50(3.0) + 70\,v_B \implies v_B = -\frac{150}{70} = -2.14\;\text{m/s (left)}
\]

\textbf{Kinetic energy released:}
\[
KE = \frac{1}{2}(50)(3.0)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(70)(2.14)^2 = 225 + 160.7 = 385.7\;\text{J}
\]

This energy came from the chemical energy in the skaters' muscles. The lighter skater moves faster, consistent with momentum conservation.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.5.4.}

\begin{solution}
Given: A 0.50\,kg clay ball moving at 8.0\,m/s strikes a 1.5\,kg clay ball moving at $-4.0$\,m/s. They stick together.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum:}
\[
m_1 v_1 + m_2 v_2 = (m_1 + m_2)v_f
\]
\[
0.50(8.0) + 1.5(-4.0) = (0.50 + 1.5)v_f
\]
\[
4.0 - 6.0 = 2.0\,v_f \implies v_f = -1.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

The combined mass moves at 1.0\,m/s in the direction of the heavier ball's initial motion.

\textbf{Energy lost:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.50)(8.0)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(1.5)(4.0)^2 = 16.0 + 12.0 = 28.0\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(2.0)(1.0)^2 = 1.0\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\text{Energy lost} = 28.0 - 1.0 = 27.0\;\text{J}\;(96.4\%\;\text{lost})
\]

This is a nearly ``maximally inelastic'' collision because the objects were moving in opposite directions and nearly stopped.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 9.6: Momentum and Collisions in Two Dimensions}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.1.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Object A ($m_A = 2.0$\,kg, $\vec{v}_{Ai} = 5.0\,\hat{i}$\,m/s) collides with Object B ($m_B = 3.0$\,kg, at rest). After collision: $\vec{v}_{Af} = 3.0\,\hat{i} + 2.0\,\hat{j}$\,m/s. Find $\vec{v}_{Bf}$.

\textbf{Conservation of momentum (vector equation):}
\[
m_A \vec{v}_{Ai} + m_B \vec{v}_{Bi} = m_A \vec{v}_{Af} + m_B \vec{v}_{Bf}
\]
\[
2.0(5.0\,\hat{i}) + 0 = 2.0(3.0\,\hat{i} + 2.0\,\hat{j}) + 3.0\,\vec{v}_{Bf}
\]
\[
10\,\hat{i} = 6.0\,\hat{i} + 4.0\,\hat{j} + 3.0\,\vec
_{Bf}
\]

\textbf{$x$-component:}
\[
10 = 6.0 + 3.0\,v_{Bf,x} \implies v_{Bf,x} = \frac{4.0}{3.0} = 1.33\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{$y$-component:}
\[
0 = 4.0 + 3.0\,v_{Bf,y} \implies v_{Bf,y} = \frac{-4.0}{3.0} = -1.33\;\text{m/s}
\]

\[
\vec{v}_{Bf} = 1.33\,\hat{i} - 1.33\,\hat{j}\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Speed of B:}
\[
|\vec{v}_{Bf}| = \sqrt{1.33^2 + 1.33^2} = 1.33\sqrt{2} = 1.89\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Direction:}
\[
\theta = \arctan\left(\frac{-1.33}{1.33}\right) = -45^\circ\;\text{(i.e., }45^\circ\text{ below the }+x\text{-axis)}
\]

\textbf{Check total momentum:}
\[
\vec{p}_f = 2.0(3.0\,\hat{i} + 2.0\,\hat{j}) + 3.0(1.33\,\hat{i} - 1.33\,\hat{j}) = (6.0 + 4.0)\,\hat{i} + (4.0 - 4.0)\,\hat{j} = 10\,\hat{i}\;\checkmark
\]
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.2.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Two objects of equal mass $m$ collide. Object 1 moves at $v_0$ in the $+x$-direction; Object 2 is at rest. After collision, Object 1 moves at angle $30^\circ$ above the $x$-axis with speed $v_1 = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}v_0$. Find the speed and direction of Object 2.

\textbf{Conservation of $x$-momentum:}
\[
mv_0 = mv_1\cos 30^\circ + mv_{2x}
\]
\[
v_0 = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}v_0 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} + v_{2x} = \frac{3}{4}v_0 + v_{2x}
\]
\[
v_{2x} = v_0 - \frac{3}{4}v_0 = \frac{1}{4}v_0
\]

\textbf{Conservation of $y$-momentum:}
\[
0 = mv_1\sin 30^\circ + mv_{2y}
\]
\[
0 = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}v_0 \cdot \frac{1}{2} + v_{2y} = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}v_0 + v_{2y}
\]
\[
v_{2y} = -\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}v_0
\]

\textbf{Speed of Object 2:}
\[
v_2 = \sqrt{v_{2x}^2 + v_{2y}^2} = \sqrt{\left(\frac{1}{4}v_0\right)^2 + \left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}v_0\right)^2} = \sqrt{\frac{1}{16}v_0^2 + \frac{3}{16}v_0^2} = \sqrt{\frac{4}{16}v_0^2} = \frac{1}{2}v_0
\]

\textbf{Direction:}
\[
\phi = \arctan\left(\frac{|\sqrt{3}/4|}{1/4}\right) = \arctan(\sqrt{3}) = 60^\circ\;\text{below the }x\text{-axis}
\]

\textbf{Check if elastic:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}mv_0^2
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}m\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}v_0\right)^2 + \frac{1}{2}m\left(\frac{1}{2}v_0\right)^2 = \frac{1}{2}m\left(\frac{3}{4}v_0^2 + \frac{1}{4}v_0^2\right) = \frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 = KE_i\;\checkmark
\]

This is an \textbf{elastic collision}, and the two objects depart at $30^\circ + 60^\circ = 90^\circ$ to each other. This $90^\circ$ separation is a general result for elastic collisions between equal masses in 2D when one is initially at rest.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem 9.6.3.}

\begin{solution}
Given: Two hockey pucks undergo a perfectly inelastic collision on frictionless ice. Puck A ($m_A = 0.20$\,kg) moves at 5.0\,m/s in the $+x$-direction. Puck B ($m_B = 0.30$\,kg) moves at 4.0\,m/s at $90^\circ$ (in the $+y$-direction). They stick together.

\textbf{Conservation of $x$-momentum:}
\[
p_x = m_A v_{Ax} + m_B(0) = 0.20(5.0) = 1.0\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Conservation of $y$-momentum:}
\[
p_y = m_A(0) + m_B v_{By} = 0.30(4.0) = 1.2\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{Final velocity:}
\[
v_{fx} = \frac{p_x}{m_A + m_B} = \frac{1.0}{0.50} = 2.0\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
v_{fy} = \frac{p_y}{m_A + m_B} = \frac{1.2}{0.50} = 2.4\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
v_f = \sqrt{2.0^2 + 2.4^2} = \sqrt{4.0 + 5.76} = \sqrt{9.76} = 3.12\;\text{m/s}
\]
\[
\theta = \arctan\left(\frac{2.4}{2.0}\right) = \arctan(1.2) = 50.2^\circ\;\text{above the }+x\text{-axis}
\]

\textbf{Energy lost:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.20)(5.0)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(0.30)(4.0)^2 = 2.5 + 2.4 = 4.9\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(0.50)(3.12)^2 = 2.44\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = 4.9 - 2.44 = 2.46\;\text{J}\;\text{lost (50.2\% lost)}
\]
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solutions for Mixed Review}

\textbf{Problem MR.1.}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Cannonball fired from a cliff:} $m = 5.0$\,kg, $v_0 = 100$\,m/s at $45^\circ$ above horizontal, cliff height $h = 50$\,m.

\textbf{Components:}
\[
v_{0x} = 100\cos 45^\circ = 70.71\;\text{m/s}, \quad v_{0y} = 100\sin 45^\circ = 70.71\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(a) Maximum height above the ground:}

Using energy conservation for the vertical direction (at max height, $v_y = 0$):
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_{0y}^2 = mg\,\Delta h \implies \Delta h = \frac{v_{0y}^2}{2g} = \frac{(70.71)^2}{2(9.8)} = \frac{5000}{19.6} = 255.1\;\text{m}
\]

Maximum height above ground: $H = h + \Delta h = 50 + 255.1 = 305.1$\,m.

\textbf{(b) Speed just before hitting the ground:}

Using energy conservation from launch to ground level (taking ground as reference):
\[
\frac{1}{2}mv_0^2 + mgh = \frac{1}{2}mv_f^2
\]
\[
v_f = \sqrt{v_0^2 + 2gh} = \sqrt{100^2 + 2(9.8)(50)} = \sqrt{10{,}000 + 980} = \sqrt{10{,}980} = 104.8\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(c) Momentum just before impact:}
\[
p = mv_f = 5.0(104.8) = 524\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(d) If the cannonball embeds in a 45\,kg target at ground level:}
\[
(m_1)v_f = (m_1 + m_2)V \implies V = \frac{5.0(104.8)}{5.0 + 45} = \frac{524}{50} = 10.48\;\text{m/s}
\]

This problem integrates projectile motion (using energy, not kinematic equations), momentum, and perfectly inelastic collisions.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem MR.2.}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Relative motion and momentum:} A 2000\,kg truck moves east at 20\,m/s. A 1000\,kg car moves west at 15\,m/s. They collide and stick together.

\textbf{(a) Velocity of car relative to truck before collision:}
\[
v_{C/T} = v_C - v_T = (-15) - (20) = -35\;\text{m/s (westward)}
\]

From the truck's perspective, the car approaches at 35\,m/s.

\textbf{(b) Final velocity after collision:}
\[
m_T v_T + m_C v_C = (m_T + m_C)v_f
\]
\[
2000(20) + 1000(-15) = 3000\,v_f
\]
\[
40{,}000 - 15{,}000 = 3000\,v_f \implies v_f = \frac{25{,}000}{3000} = 8.33\;\text{m/s east}
\]

\textbf{(c) Kinetic energy lost:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(2000)(20)^2 + \frac{1}{2}(1000)(15)^2 = 400{,}000 + 112{,}500 = 512{,}500\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(3000)(8.33)^2 = 104{,}167\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = 512{,}500 - 104{,}167 = 408{,}333\;\text{J}\;\text{(79.7\% lost)}
\]

\textbf{(d) Impulse on the car:}
\[
J_C = m_C(v_f - v_{Ci}) = 1000(8.33 - (-15)) = 1000(23.33) = 23{,}333\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s (eastward)}
\]

\textbf{Impulse on the truck:}
\[
J_T = m_T(v_f - v_{Ti}) = 2000(8.33 - 20) = 2000(-11.67) = -23{,}333\;\text{N}\cdot\text{s (westward)}
\]

Note: $J_C = -J_T$, confirming Newton's third law.
\end{solution}

\textbf{Problem MR.3.}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Circular motion and momentum:} A 0.50\,kg ball on a 1.0\,m string moves in a horizontal circle at 4.0\,m/s. The string breaks and the ball flies off tangentially, colliding with a 1.5\,kg block at rest on a frictionless surface. The collision is perfectly inelastic.

\textbf{(a) Centripetal acceleration and tension before the string breaks:}
\[
a_c = \frac{v^2}{r} = \frac{16}{1.0} = 16\;\text{m/s}^2
\]
\[
T = ma_c = 0.50(16) = 8.0\;\text{N}
\]

(We are ignoring gravity's effect on the string here, treating this as a horizontal circle.)

\textbf{(b) Momentum of ball just after string breaks:}

The ball continues with the same speed (tangential velocity):
\[
p_{\text{ball}} = mv = 0.50(4.0) = 2.0\;\text{kg}\cdot\text{m/s (tangential direction)}
\]

\textbf{(c) Velocity after perfectly inelastic collision with block:}
\[
m_{\text{ball}}\,v_{\text{ball}} = (m_{\text{ball}} + m_{\text{block}})v_f
\]
\[
0.50(4.0) = (0.50 + 1.5)v_f \implies v_f = \frac{2.0}{2.0} = 1.0\;\text{m/s}
\]

\textbf{(d) Energy lost in collision:}
\[
KE_i = \frac{1}{2}(0.50)(4.0)^2 = 4.0\;\text{J}
\]
\[
KE_f = \frac{1}{2}(2.0)(1.0)^2 = 1.0\;\text{J}
\]
\[
\Delta KE = 4.0 - 1.0 = 3.0\;\text{J}\;\text{(75\% lost)}
\]

This problem integrates uniform circular motion, tangential velocity, conservation of momentum, and energy analysis in an inelastic collision.
\end{solution}

%========================================================
% APPENDIX: QUICK REFERENCE GUIDE
%========================================================

\appendix

\section{Quick Reference Guide}

\subsection{Key Definitions}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Impulse ($\vec{J}$):} The product of force and the time interval over which it acts; $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t$. Impulse equals the change in momentum.

\item \textbf{Momentum ($\vec{p}$):} The product of an object's mass and velocity; $\vec{p} = m\vec{v}$. A vector quantity representing ``quantity of motion.''

\item \textbf{Impulse-Momentum Theorem:} $\vec{J} = \Delta\vec{p} = m\vec{v}_f - m\vec{v}_i$.

\item \textbf{Conservation of Momentum:} In an isolated system (no external forces), total momentum is constant: $\vec{p}_i = \vec{p}_f$.

\item \textbf{Elastic Collision:} A collision in which both momentum and kinetic energy are conserved.

\item \textbf{Inelastic Collision:} A collision in which momentum is conserved but kinetic energy is not. In a \emph{perfectly inelastic} collision, the objects stick together.

\item \textbf{Velocity ($\vec{v}$):} The rate of change of position; $\vec{v} = \Delta\vec{x}/\Delta t$ (average) or $\vec{v} = d\vec{x}/dt$ (instantaneous).

\item \textbf{Acceleration ($\vec{a}$):} The rate of change of velocity; $\vec{a} = \Delta\vec{v}/\Delta t$ (average) or $\vec{a} = d\vec{v}/dt$ (instantaneous).

\item \textbf{Average Velocity:} For constant acceleration, $v_{\text{avg}} = (v_i + v_f)/2$.

\item \textbf{Projectile Motion:} Motion under gravity alone, with constant horizontal velocity and constant vertical acceleration $g = 9.8$\,m/s$^2$ downward.

\item \textbf{Centripetal Acceleration:} Acceleration directed toward the center of a circular path; $a_c = v^2/r$.

\item \textbf{Relative Velocity:} The velocity of object A as measured by observer B; $\vec{v}_{A/B} = \vec{v}_{A/G} - \vec{v}_{B/G}$.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Important Formulas and Relationships}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.6}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\
\arraystretch}{1.6}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{7.5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Concept} & \textbf{Formula / Relationship} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Concept} & \textbf{Formula / Relationship} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{2}{r}{\textit{Continued on next page}} \\
\bottomrule
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot

Momentum & $\vec{p} = m\vec{v}$ \\

Impulse & $\vec{J} = \vec{F}_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t$ \\

Impulse-Momentum Theorem & $\vec{J} = \Delta\vec{p} = m\vec{v}_f - m\vec{v}_i$ \\

Conservation of Momentum & $m_1\vec{v}_{1i} + m_2\vec{v}_{2i} = m_1\vec{v}_{1f} + m_2\vec{v}_{2f}$ \\

Perfectly Inelastic Collision & $m_1 v_{1i} + m_2 v_{2i} = (m_1 + m_2)v_f$ \\

Elastic Collision (1-D, target at rest) & $v_{1f} = \dfrac{m_1 - m_2}{m_1 + m_2}\,v_{1i}$;\quad $v_{2f} = \dfrac{2m_1}{m_1 + m_2}\,v_{1i}$ \\

Kinetic Energy & $KE = \dfrac{1}{2}mv^2 = \dfrac{p^2}{2m}$ \\

Definition of Velocity & $\vec{v}_{\text{avg}} = \dfrac{\Delta\vec{x}}{\Delta t}$ \\

Definition of Acceleration & $\vec{a}_{\text{avg}} = \dfrac{\Delta\vec{v}}{\Delta t}$ \\

Average Velocity (constant $a$) & $v_{\text{avg}} = \dfrac{v_i + v_f}{2}$ \\

Displacement (constant $a$) & $\Delta x = v_{\text{avg}}\,\Delta t = \dfrac{v_i + v_f}{2}\,\Delta t$ \\

Velocity from Energy & $v_f^2 = v_i^2 + 2a\,\Delta x$ \quad (derived from energy) \\

Projectile: Horizontal & $x = v_{0x}\,t$;\quad $v_x = v_{0x} = v_0\cos\theta$ \\

Projectile: Vertical & $v_y = v_{0y} - gt$;\quad $v_{0y} = v_0\sin\theta$ \\

Range of Projectile & $R = \dfrac{v_0^2\sin 2\theta}{g}$ \quad (level ground) \\

Maximum Height & $h = \dfrac{v_{0y}^2}{2g}$ \\

Centripetal Acceleration & $a_c = \dfrac{v^2}{r} = \dfrac{4\pi^2 r}{T^2}$ \\

Period of Circular Motion & $T = \dfrac{2\pi r}{v}$ \\

Relative Velocity & $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ \\

Work-Energy Theorem & $W_{\text{net}} = \Delta KE$ \\

Conservation of Energy & $KE_i + PE_i = KE_f + PE_f$ \quad (no non-conservative work) \\

\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Problem-Solving Method: The Unified Approach}

The following method integrates all the tools from this unit. For \textbf{any} mechanics problem, follow these steps:

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{The Unified Problem-Solving Framework}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:},leftmargin=2.5cm]
\item \textbf{Identify the system and draw a diagram.} Label all objects, velocities, forces, and relevant distances or angles.

\item \textbf{Identify the time intervals.} Break the problem into phases (e.g., before collision, during collision, after collision; launch phase, flight phase, landing phase).

\item \textbf{For each phase, choose the right principle:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Collision or explosion?} $\rightarrow$ Use \textbf{conservation of momentum}.
\item \textbf{Object moving under forces with no collision?} $\rightarrow$ Use \textbf{conservation of energy} and/or \textbf{Newton's second law} (forces $\rightarrow$ acceleration).
\item \textbf{Need to find time?} $\rightarrow$ Use the \textbf{definitions of velocity and acceleration} (not kinematic equations---use $v_{\text{avg}} = \frac{v_i+v_f}{2}$ and $a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t}$).
\item \textbf{Need to find distance without time?} $\rightarrow$ Use \textbf{energy methods} ($W_{\text{net}} = \Delta KE$, or $KE_i + PE_i = KE_f + PE_f$).
\item \textbf{Need impulse or force during brief contact?} $\rightarrow$ Use the \textbf{impulse-momentum theorem}.
\end{itemize}

\item \textbf{Apply the chosen principle.} Write the equation, substitute known values, and solve for the unknown.

\item \textbf{Check your answer.} Verify units, check limiting cases, and ensure the answer is physically reasonable. If possible, verify using an alternative method.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\subsection{When to Use Each Tool --- Decision Table}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Situation} & \textbf{Primary Tool} & \textbf{Key Equation} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Situation} & \textbf{Primary Tool} & \textbf{Key Equation} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot

Collision (any type) & Conservation of Momentum & $\sum m_i\vec{v}_i = \sum m_f\vec{v}_f$ \\

Elastic collision & Momentum + Energy & Add $KE_i = KE_f$ \\

Perfectly inelastic collision & Momentum (objects stick) & $(m_1+m_2)v_f = m_1v_{1i}+m_2v_{2i}$ \\

Explosion / recoil & Momentum ($p_i = 0$) & $0 = m_1v_1 + m_2v_2$ \\

Finding force during collision & Impulse-Momentum & $F_{\text{avg}} = \Delta p / \Delta t$ \\

Object in free fall (no time needed) & Energy Conservation & $\frac{1}{2}mv_i^2 + mgh_i = \frac{1}{2}mv_f^2 + mgh_f$ \\

Object in free fall (time needed) & Definitions of $v$ and $a$ & $v_{\text{avg}} = \frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}$; $a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t}$ \\

Projectile motion & Decompose into $x$ and $y$ & $x$: constant $v$; $y$: constant $a = -g$ \\

Circular motion & Centripetal acceleration & $a_c = v^2/r$; $T = 2\pi r/v$ \\

Relative motion & Vector addition & $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$ \\

Constant acceleration (find $v_f$ without $t$) & Energy or $v^2$ relation & $v_f^2 = v_i^2 + 2a\Delta x$ \\

Constant acceleration (find $\Delta x$ with $t$) & Average velocity & $\Delta x = \frac{v_i + v_f}{2}\,t$ \\

\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Study Checklist}

Before moving to the next unit, make sure you can:

\begin{itemize}[label=$\square$]
\item Define impulse, momentum, and the impulse-momentum theorem in your own words
\item Calculate the impulse delivered to an object from force and time, or from change in momentum
\item Apply conservation of momentum to collisions and explosions in one and two dimensions
\item Distinguish between elastic, inelastic, and perfectly inelastic collisions and identify which quantities are conserved in each
\item Solve ballistic pendulum and two-stage collision-plus-energy problems
\item Decompose 2-D collision problems into independent $x$ and $y$ momentum equations
\item Describe motion with constant acceleration using the definitions of velocity and acceleration (not kinematic equations)
\item Use average velocity $v_{\text{avg}} = (v_i + v_f)/2$ and the definition of acceleration $a = \Delta v/\Delta t$ to solve one-dimensional motion problems
\item Decompose two-dimensional motion into independent horizontal and vertical components
\item Solve projectile motion problems by treating horizontal (constant velocity) and vertical (constant acceleration) motions independently
\item Apply energy conservation as an alternative to kinematic equations for finding speeds and distances
\item Calculate centripetal acceleration and relate speed, radius, and period for uniform circular motion
\item Add velocities using the relative velocity equation $\vec{v}_{A/C} = \vec{v}_{A/B} + \vec{v}_{B/C}$
\item Solve river-crossing, airplane-wind, and moving-reference-frame problems
\item Choose the correct tool (momentum, energy, forces, definitions) for each phase of a multi-step problem
\item Verify answers using alternative methods and dimensional analysis
\end{itemize}

\end{document}
